Preface 
 
This thesis was prepared as a culmination of the master’s degree at The Technical University 
of Denmark (DTU). Throughout our study at DTU, we have become aware of the importance 
of acting against climate change. In our line of study, civil engineering, it was clear that many 
initiatives already have been made. However, we found a lack of existing practices within the 
load-bearing structure regarding a circular approach for material use. 
In this study, we investigated the procedure of separating hollow core slabs (HCSs) using De-
sign for Disassembly (DfD) methods. Specifically using hydraulics to separate HCSs grouted 
with lower-strength mortar is investigated. Implementing DfD should ease separation and pro-
mote the reuse of HCSs. The study was performed with an experimental, economical, and prac-
tical approach. The significance of the thesis lies in the new knowledge gained in the field of 
implementing DfD of HCSs. 
We would like to give our acknowledgments to our supervisor Philip Skov Halding who has 
been an excellent support with a broad knowledge in the field of concrete structures and DfD. 
Furthermore, we would like to thank his colleague Wolfgang Kunther.  
Gratitude must also be given to all the interviewees for their time and valuable input. Moreover, 
thanks are given to NCC Denmark and Boligbeton for providing access to data on HCSs.  
We would also like to extend our gratefulness to DTU Construct for their expertise and help in 
the casting- and testing facilities. In addition, a humble thank you should be given to STARK 
Glostrup for their donation of cement. 
Lastly, special appreciation is given to Wissenberg A/S for supporting the project financially.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:6==
 
 
 
vi 
 
Abstract 
 
The construction industry plays a major role in terms of impact on the carbon footprint through 
activities such as the production of precast concrete involving cement manufacturing. Addi-
tionally, the construction industry accounts for a significant amount of global waste production. 
One promising solution that has started to gain recognition in the industry is the implementation 
of Design for Disassembly (DfD). The potential of DfD and reuse lies in reducing the con-
sumption of non-renewable resources, and CO2 emissions from extraction, manufacturing, and 
disposal of building materials. Currently, DfD is primarily exploited within the building trades, 
but not yet for the traditional load-bearing structures. At the moment, no methods can effec-
tively separate grouted concrete elements without causing damage to the hollow core slabs 
(HCSs) while remaining economically competitive against a demolishing scenario.  
This thesis aims to investigate the potential as well as the feasibility of reusing HCSs through 
a DfD concept. The idea for the DfD concept is to grout HCSs using a lower-strength mortar 
than the elements. When force is applied to the structure, the formation of cracks should occur 
in the lower-strength grout or in the interface between the HCSs and the lower-strength grout. 
Thus, the HCSs could potentially be separated using hydraulic pumps to achieve shear failure 
and avoid damaging the HCSs during disassembly.  
Based on the literature, it is assumed that DfD using hydraulics and lower-strength mortar is 
feasible. The method is further confirmed to be plausible by comparative and empirical anal-
yses throughout the report. This thesis suggests three DfD proposals to specifically answer 
barriers found in Eurocode 1992: Integrating reuse into project design, employing lower-
strength mortars for grouting, and eliminating reinforcement inside hollow cores in HCSs. 
Multiple shear tests have been performed on specimens to reflect separating HCSs subjected 
to horizontal shear force along the edge of the grouting surrounding HCSs. The specimens 
consisted of two different cementitious materials cast together to generate an interface between 
them. C45 concrete was used to simulate the HCSs, and was combined with a lower-strength 
mortar, varying in type of mortar, degree of reinforcement, and presence of normal force. Three 
different mortars were used, consisting of two lime-cement mortars (KC) and one cement-
based mortar. Reinforcement was added to simulate the proposed DfD suggestions. 
The structural behavior of the various configurations was investigated by analyzing load-dis-
placement relations when subjected to shear force. The specimens were further analyzed with 
Digital Image Correlation (DIC) monitoring displacements of the specimens. The capacities of 
the test results were compared with relevant theory to approximate their applicability to a dis-
assembly scenario. The cement-based mortar correlated well with theory in contrast to the lime-
cement based mortars which failed to induce yielding of the reinforcement. These observations 
were related to the lower strength of the lime-cement mortars which generated a high degree 
of reinforcement. The high degree of reinforcement resulted in reduced shear capacities of the 
specimens compared to the theoretical estimated shear capacities. Moreover, the influence of 
lime resulted in a significantly slower strength-development compared to the cement-based 
mortar. Combined with the previous point, it was considered that lime-cement mortars may not 
be an ideal replacement for grout in a DfD scenario.  
==PAGE:7==
 
 
 
vii 
 
Due to lack of existing costs for reused HCSs, a project case for Building 310 at DTU was 
calculated. The case involved an estimate for traditional demolishing and demounting of ele-
ments to determine the economic impact of DfD on HCSs. It was found that demounting could 
meet the same time frame as demolishing. The estimates indicate that demounting without DfD 
was four times costlier than demolishing, whereas demounting with DfD was only three times 
more expensive. However, the estimates did not include the potential resale value of the reused 
HCSs. The reselling price of reused HCSs was determined on a basis to make the total cost of 
demounting as low as the total cost of demolishing. 
The pricing of a demounted HCS without DfD was then estimated to be approximately 1.5 
times the cost of a new HCS while demounting HCSs with DfD should be sold for the same 
price as new HCSs. The case estimate suggested promising economic results for the implemen-
tation of HCSs in the circular economy.  
 
Keywords: Sustainability, Concrete structures, Hollow core slabs (HCSs), Reuse, Demount-
ing, Design for Disassembly (DfD), Lower-strength mortar, Hydraulic pumps, Experimental 
test, Shear test, Digital Image Correlation (DIC), Economic estimates, Mapping of protocols

xii

1 
 
Prefabricated construction is a widely used construction method in the building industry. The 
average lifespan of a commercial building is around 60 years which typically ends with demol-
ishing [1]. Around 90% of the concrete is recycled as crushed concrete, primarily for use as 
road fill [2]. Hence, there is a significant potential for reuse of prefabricated concrete elements.  
This thesis explores the application of Design for Disassembly (DfD) principles limited to the 
context of prefabricated hollow core slabs (HCSs). 
The concept of DfD has started to make its way into the construction industry. However, pri-
marily for elements with dry connections, as it is more accessible in contrast to wet connec-
tions. DfD of wet joints has not yet been widely implemented, due to its more complex nature 
[3]. Hence, it is necessary to implement new innovative methods, which is the objective of this 
study. 
The study focuses on the idea of separating deck elements using hydraulic pumps. To improve 
this procedure, lower-strength mortars are investigated for use as grouting between the deck 
elements. Using mortars as grout that have lower strength than the deck element itself is ex-
pected to have the failure occur in the grout or in the interface during separation. Thus, the deck 
element will remain entire and is applicable for reuse.  
This thesis undertakes a comprehensive investigation including experimental, economic, and 
practical aspects of the subject matter. To address the topic, qualitative interview sessions were 
conducted, along with conducting low-scale shear tests. Additionally, data analysis of recently 
installed HCSs and case analysis were performed. 
During experimental tests, a total of 72 specimens were loaded until failure. The specimens 
consisted of a C45 concrete, imitating a deck element, combined with three different lower-
strength mortars, imitating the grouting. The lower-strength mortars consisted of one cement-
based mortar and two lime-cement mortars (KC). The specimens varied with different degrees 
of reinforcement and half of the specimens were applied with 10 kN normal force. During the 
analysis of the tests, a range of tools were employed. These included Autodesk Inventor Pro-
fessional 2023, GOM Correlate, and diagrams. 
The following subchapters 1.1, 1.2, 1.3, and 1.4 are further elaborating the thesis as well as the 
main objectives. 
 
1 
Introduction
==PAGE:14==
Introduction 
 
1.1 Motivation of study 
2 
 
1.1 
Motivation of study  
The following are unavoidable statements that the building sector must address.  
• The construction industry is a significant contributor to CO2 emissions, accounting for 
30% of CO2 production and 35% of all waste in Denmark [4]. 
• The world is slowly depleted of resources used in the production of concrete [5]. 
• The government is increasing the requirements to reduce CO2 emissions in the con-
struction industry [6]. 
The following opportunities are arguments that help form the motivation of this study. 
• Embracing circular economy principles can address resource challenges worldwide and 
provide economic benefits for Denmark and its businesses [7]. 
• HCSs have been identified as one of the most feasible prefabricated elements to reuse 
currently [8]. 
By considering these statements, the study aims to contribute to the sustainable reuse of HCSs, 
addressing environmental concerns, resource depletion, and evolving industry requirements. 
 
1.2 
Structure of the report  
The idea of the thesis is to both combine a theoretical- and practical-oriented study. This chap-
ter is intended to outline the structure of this thesis. The composition is structured around six 
key phases as shown in Figure 1.1.

==PAGE:15==
Introduction 
 
1.3 Objectives of the thesis 
3 
 
As seen in Figure 1.1, the research phase is based on a literature review that explores existing 
scholarly work, scientific papers, and relevant publications. Interviewing stakeholders strives 
to gain valuable inputs that offer practical context and enrich the research findings. Empirical 
analysis of HCSs aims to assess their performance and characteristics. Based on the back-
ground phase, a proposal is developed to create a DfD-system. The proposal is then examined 
through extensive experimental tests. Following the experimental tests, an evaluation of the 
real-world scenarios, assessing aspects like reusability and cost is evaluated. The overall report 
ends with a conclusion wrapping up the final insights. 
 
1.3 
Objectives of the thesis 
The main objective of this thesis is to develop a suitable DfD-system for HCSs with a focus on 
practical and economic implementation. To achieve this objective, the following aspects will 
be investigated throughout the report: 
• Identifying the most effective sustainable solution for HCSs by comparing recycling, 
repurposing, and reusing options in terms of their impact on Global Warming Potential 
(GWP). 
• Investigating the potential of DfD-techniques using lower-strength mortars and hydrau-
lics to separate HCSs. 
• Assessing the feasibility of DfD solutions by conducting a comprehensive analysis of 
literature, stakeholder interviews, and empirical examination of HCSs to overcome crit-
ical barriers to reuse of HCSs. 
• Exploring the impact of different mortars in shear tests, both with and without rein-
forcement and normal force. 
• Conducting an economic evaluation to assess the cost implications of demounting 
HCSs, both with and without DfD, considering the potential resale value of reused 
HCSs. 
 
1.4 
Scope and limitations  
The focus of this report is based on the following scope and limitations. 
 
SCOPE: 
• The focus is on reusing HCSs exclusively, not other prefabricated concrete elements. 
• The focus is on utilizing hydraulics for disassembly to minimize the cutting of elements. 
• The focus is to use lower-strength mortars grout between hollow core slabs. 
• The focus is to use a realistic approach with proposals and experimental tests that are 
simulating standard designs from Eurocode 1992. 
 
==PAGE:16==
Introduction 
 
1.4 Scope and limitations 
4 
 
LIMITATIONS: 
• Literature is limited by the available data.  
• This thesis uses material strengths determined in a report by Cømert [9], further inves-
tigations should be required. 
• This thesis assumes that hydraulic disassembly of lower-strength mortar is possible 
based on the outcome of the two reports by Shrestha [10] and by Sharaf [11], further 
investigations should be required
 
==PAGE:17==
 
 
 
5 
 
This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive review of the existing literature within the field 
and evaluate crucial subjects that are essential for the development of this research. By exam-
ining the current state-of-the-art practices, areas of improvement or potential gaps in 
knowledge can be identified. 
One of the primary areas covered in this literature review is the standard design criteria for 
hollow core slabs (HCSs). Understanding the established norms and guidelines is essential for 
ensuring the structural integrity, durability, and safety of HCSs in civil engineering projects. 
In addition, this literature review will also delve into the methodology of life cycle analysis 
(LCA) and compare the global warming potential (GWP) associated with various approaches 
to recycling, repurposing, and reusing concrete. Lastly, the literature review will examine the 
use of hydraulic pumps and lower-strength mortars to separate HCSs while controlling the 
formation of cracks in the construction joints between HCSs. 
 
2.1 
Current design of hollow core slabs 
The basic construction technique for a building made of prefabricated concrete elements con-
sists of beams, columns, walls, and HCSs that are connected in various types of joints in the 
building system. What these all have in common is that the floor deck acts as a disk, usually 
formed by HCSs [12]. HCSs are precast elements of pre-stressed concrete in the longitudinal 
direction. They vary in type, thickness, width, and amount of pre-stressed wires depending on 
span length and acting loads.  
 
2.1.1 
Publications 
HSCs undergo thorough calculation methods with extensive requirements, and quality assur-
ance procedures to ensure structural integrity and durability. By referencing industry standards 
and complying with local building codes, engineers and construction professionals can design 
and construct HCSs that meet safety standards and industry best practices. The following de-
scription in Table 2.1 highlights the procedural aspects involved in the design and construction 
of HCSs. 
 
 
2 
Literature review

Table 2.1 provides an overview of the most relevant publications to this thesis, many more 
publications are also involved or as referenced in the standards. The load-carrying capacities 
are based on DS/EN 1992-1 while the product-specific dimensioning rules for HCSs are based 
on DS/EN 1168.  
This thesis aims to provide a DfD-system, thus it is relevant to highlight Annex G in DS/EN 
1168 which is elaborated in DS/EN 1992-1-2 as well as highlighting Annex H in DS/EN 1168. 
These two Annexes make up the framework for standard design solutions on the market as will 
be shown in chapter 2.1.2. 
In Denmark there is only a standard for the production of HCSs, a standard for reuse of HCSs 
is not existing yet. Norway on the other hand, has issued NS 3682:2022 [13], as a publication 
that describes a method for documentation of properties on a level that corresponds to the basis 
for issuing a statement of performance (CE-mark) to EN 1168. 
 
2.1.2 
Standard designs 
The typical design widths of HCSs are 1.2 m and 2.4 m as shown in Figure 2.1. 
 
(a) 
 
(b) 
Figure 2.1: inspired by DS/EN 1992-1-1:  
Standard element widths for HCS 1.2 m (a) and 2.4 m (b) 
==PAGE:19==
Literature review 
2.1 Current design of hollow core slabs 
7 
 
HCSs are individual concrete elements, and when put together they are designed to act as one 
whole slab that can obtain and redistribute horizontal loads to stabilize walls below. The most 
widespread method of achieving one full disc effect of the HCSs is to use reinforcement as 
stringers between decks which is grouted with in situ concrete as described in DS/EN 1992-1-
1. Notice the space between the 1.2 m HCS which must be filled with grout Figure 2.1 (a), 
while the 2.4 m wide HCS (b) has an extra hollow core where the grout between decks would 
have been. 
Stringer plans are project specific and can include special designs for reinforcement solutions. 
This could for instance be beams that are incorporated in the stringer reinforcement or treaded 
rods that are clamped in an element. These solutions were designed by the engineers on the 
project. For that reason, it is deemed necessary to focus on the standard requirements and con-
nections in this thesis, as they are necessary for any multi-story construction instead of project-
specific solutions that are unique. 
A structural engineer from MOE noted in Appendix A.3 that the most common standard details 
for reinforcement design to focus on are from minimum fire- and robustness requirements as 
elaborated in DS/EN 1992-1-1. The requirements for horizontal- and vertical ties between 
HCSs are ensured by following the practices as shown in Figure 2.2.  
 
Figure 2.2: inspired by DS/EN 1992-1-1: Minimum joint requirements. 
*indicate dimensions that are project specific 
D1 and D2 are dictated following DS/EN 1168. Furthermore, D3 as shown in Figure 2.2 rep-
resents a standard design solution using hammerheads, they are usually added to obtain trans-
verse shear stresses and tie the deck together to form one singular acting slab.  
A typical hammerhead can be seen in Figure 2.3. 
==PAGE:20==
Literature review 
2.1 Current design of hollow core slabs 
8 
 
 
Figure 2.3: Photo by Anders Rafn:  
A hammerhead in a project 
As seen in Figure 2.3 the recess is formed as a hammerhead, hence the name. Hammerheads 
and stringers in all directions are calculated by the engineers for each project and are thus 
varying. Rebars that are marked by * in Figure 2.2 indicates the minimum design requirements. 
Stringers must always be closed in any longitudinal end by rebars in a size equal to the largest 
stringer connected.  
The details D1 and D2 are shown below in Figure 2.4 and Figure 2.5.  
 
 
 
(a) 
(b) 
Figure 2.4: Inspired by CHR Concrete [14]:  
Standard detail for D1 (a) and D2 (b) 
 
Figure 2.4 shows detail D1 and D2. The challenge with HSCs is that if a fire occurs, the heat 
can be directed into the channels from below and thereby warm up the pre-tensioned wires and 
reinforcement according to DS/EN 1992-1-1.  
==PAGE:21==
Literature review 
2.1 Current design of hollow core slabs 
9 
 
To ensure a good anchoring of the joint reinforcement against shear in the deck elements during 
the fire, the reinforcement in the longitudinal joints should at least comply with the following 
requirements as shown in D1 and D2 in Figure 2.2 and Figure 2.4. Joint reinforcement in the 
longitudinal direction must always have at least one Y10 U-bar as D1 or one straight Y12 rebar 
as D2 at deck edges per 1.2 m. Both the joint reinforcement for straight rebars Y12 and U-bars 
Y10 must be inserted at least a distance of la = 1.5 m into the longitudinal joint and grouted. 
 
Detail D3 is shown in Figure 2.5. The pur-
pose of the joint reinforcement is to obtain 
horizontal shear stress along the perimeter 
reinforcement. It is not a requirement to use 
hammerheads, but it has become a common 
solution to obtain shear stresses, and as seen 
as a standard solution at most manufacturers. 
The shear stresses depend on the layout of 
the floor diaphragm and reinforcement de-
sign; thus, they can vary, and the engineers 
must assess them individually. However, as 
it has become a common practice to use, 
most suppliers have standard solutions for 
size and distances based on experiences [15].  
 
Figure 2.5: Inspired by CHR Concrete [14]: 
Standard detail D3 
 
This thesis aims to provide a DfD-system in Chapter 5 for these three details, because they are 
expected in any construction using HCSs. 
 
2.1.3 Quality control of reused HCSs 
The process of implementing the reuse of HCSs is being investigated. However, ensuring qual-
ity control for reused HCSs is difficult due to lack of documentation, and varied conditions 
from the use phase combined with HCSs originating from different projects and manufacturers 
[16]. 
Lendager Group and DTU Sustain have published a midway conference for “Structural Reuse” 
[17]. This publication provides perspectives on documentation for quality control of reused 
HCSs. The publication provides an overview of implemented standards in Denmark which are 
presented in Figure 2.6 below. 
==PAGE:22==
Literature review 
2.1 Current design of hollow core slabs 
10 
 
 
Figure 2.6: From the midway conference for “Structural Reuse” [17]:  
Barriers to reuse of building components. A white circle represents a barrier. The more saturated the 
white circle, the more important the barrier is considered  
The circle in Figure 2.6 is divided into four key themes with each document type allocated 
within. Joint technical property establishes documentation requirements for buildings. Regula-
tory requirements at the product level cover detailed documentation such as load-bearing ca-
pacity, fire resistance, and structural integrity of HCSs. Standard terms of processes provide 
standardized contract terms for services and define responsibilities between clients and stake-
holders. Voluntary schemes provide systems that can be used for certifications such as DGNB. 
In Figure 2.6, the midway publication highlights the following documents that are significant 
barriers that need to be addressed before the implementation of HCS reuse can be realized: 
Eurocodes, CE marking, Declaration of Performance (DoP), DS/Insta Nordic standards, and 
DS standards. It should be noted that all these documents are extensively referenced in BR18, 
which represents legal requirements. However, currently, they do not support the reuse of 
HCSs.  
==PAGE:23==
Literature review 
2.2 Life cycle analysis 
11 
 
From the midway publication, two main outtakes for future implementation of reused HCSs 
were to use multiple categorization schemes and nondestructive test (NDT) to control and val-
idate the reused HCSs [17]. 
It is outside the scope of this thesis to further comprehend all these documents from Figure 2.6, 
but this thesis aims to provide valuable insights from both theoretical research and practical 
application that hopefully can have a positive influence on the documents. 
 
2.2 
Life cycle analysis  
The concept of sustainability has become a crucial aspect of the building sector, and in recent 
years, the circular economy approach has gained significant attention. The circular economy 
aims to reduce waste and maximize the value of resources. Precast concrete elements are 
widely used in the building sector, and the sustainability of their production, use, and disposal 
has become a critical issue [18]. 
This literary research found that specifically repurposing, recycling, and reusing proved to be 
the most relevant and suitable regarding precast concrete structures. Table 2.2 presents a defi-
nition of the three terms to underline the differences. 
Table 2.2: Definition of recycling, repurposing, and reusing 
 
 
Recycle: 
“Recovery and reprocessing of waste materials for use in new materials.” [19] 
Repurpose: 
“To reuse something for a different purpose from the one that was originally 
intended.” [20] 
Reuse: 
“[…] using of the object or material again and again for the same purpose or 
for different purpose without altering the form of the product.” [21] 
 
Table 2.2 outlines a huge difference between recycling and reuse that is important to keep in 
mind. The master thesis provides a solution for the reuse of HCSs. The following subchapter 
2.2.1 to 2.2.5 evaluates and compares the three different sustainable alternatives.  
 
2.2.1 
Environmental product declarations 
A comprehensive literature review was conducted to identify the existing knowledge and un-
derstanding of sustainable solutions for precast concrete structures. To evaluate the difference 
in outcome from each method, Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) is used with Environmental Prod-
uct Declarations (EPDs) as data.  
EPD uses a flow diagram to describe the different stages of a product as seen in Figure 2.7:  
 
==PAGE:24==
Literature review 
2.2 Life cycle analysis 
12 
 
 
Figure 2.7: EPD Flow diagram inspired by EPD Danmark [22] 
EPD and other environmental papers give specific insight into precast concrete elements and 
HCSs regarding the method of recycling and reuse. However, repurpose is much more difficult 
to evaluate, thus numbers regarding single-family house were used in comparisons, resulting 
in numbers that also take installation, etc. into account.  
Many factors are involved to describe the effective result of each method; thus, the comparison 
is focused on Global Warming Potential (GWP) results only from acknowledged publishers, 
which allows for specific numerical outcomes to compare. The methods range from different 
stages across the EPD flow diagram as described in the upcoming calculations. 
 
The following subsections compare some of the current most used sustainable solutions for 
transforming concrete waste. 
 
2.2.2 Recycle 
Recycle is when waste is processed and used as or in new materials. Concrete is obvious to use 
as an end-of-life product as it can be crushed and transformed into road fill. According to EPD 
Danmark [2], more than 90% of concrete waste is recycled, with the majority being used as 
stabilizing layers under roads in Denmark. 
Using EPD Danmark [2], the GWP impact of one m2 320 mm thick HCS, being demolished 
from an existing building and crushed to use as road fill following category C1-D as seen in 
Figure 2.7, is calculated in equation (2.1):

Gravel in roads is a mined resource from category A1-A3 as seen in Figure 2.7. According to

Thus, crushed concrete used as road fill instead of mined gravel for road fill results in a CO2 
reduction of 18% when comparing equations (2.1) and (2.3). 
 
Alternatives 
Crushed concrete as road fill only has an 18% reduction of CO2 emissions. Hence, it is im-
portant to consider possible alternative use of crushed concrete. This chapter investigates the 
environmental impact of using crushed concrete as an aggregate in new concrete as well. Two 
reports have been investigated, which conclude two different results.  
The first report is by Miljøstyrelsen, which examines crushed concrete as aggregate at a con-
crete factory [24]. EPD of passive concrete with 20 MPa compressive strength, P20, conven-
tional concrete, without crushed concrete as aggregate, follows product stages A1-A4 and has 
the following impact as equation (2.4) shows: 
 
222.00 kg CO2
m3
 
(2.4) 
 
EPD of P20 concrete with crushed concrete from the same report by Miljøstyrelsen as an ag-
gregate is seen in equation (2.5) [24]: 
 
231.00 kg CO2
m3
 
(2.5) 
 
By comparing equation (2.4) with (2.5), it is found that crushed concrete as aggregate actually 
increases the CO2 footprint by 4.5%.  
 
==PAGE:26==
Literature review 
2.2 Life cycle analysis 
14 
 
The difference is because more cement is required to ensure the same strength and properties 
of the new concrete mix, as compared to using normal aggregates. Cement production accounts 
for approximately 90% of the total CO2 footprint of concrete [25]. These emissions arise from 
the energy consumed during the kiln heating process and the chemical reactions that occur 
during the conversion of chalk to cement clinker. Thus, a minimal increase in cement content 
has a greater impact on reducing the consumption of earth's resources than decreasing the use 
of aggregates. 
As the annual production of new concrete in Denmark varies, so does the amount of concrete 
that is demolished. According to a report by DTU called BYG R-371, the available amount of 
demolished concrete can only account for 15-30% of the necessary aggregate needed to pro-
duce new concrete [26]. This indicates that production cannot keep up with the demand for 
new construction with the use of recycled concrete alone. Which also underlines the importance 
of developing new solutions.  
The second report by Miljøstyrelsen has made a specific case about building a Pelican® Stor-
age. This project used crushed concrete as aggregate being mixed on the site, which gave a 
reduction of 11% in CO2 emissions compared to standard concrete with normal aggregates 
[27].  
The use of crushed concrete as aggregate in new concrete can increase the CO2 footprint due 
to the stages A1-A4 in the life cycle analysis. However, if the concrete is crushed from the 
existing building on site and the mixing of new concrete is carried out on site as the Pelican 
Storage case does [27], the CO2 footprint can be reduced by using stages C1, D1, and A3. It is 
important to note that this approach is highly dependent on the specific project and its aim to 
achieve sustainability. Therefore, it may not be easily replicable in other projects, whereas us-
ing crushed concrete as aggregate in a factory setting is a more feasible solution to expand 
production. 
 
To determine the most effective approach to reducing the CO2 footprint or limiting the deple-
tion of the world's resources, further investigation is required. The availability of crushed con-
crete is limited, and this makes concrete ineffective as aggregates. As a result, it is assessed 
that crushed concrete is more feasible for use as road gravel. 
 
2.2.3 Repurpose  
Repurposing a building to serve another purpose requires renovation. Renovating allows for 
modifications and improvements to be made to the existing structure to either change or en-
hance its functionality. A comparison for renovation in a single-family house is made in equa-
tion (2.6) and (2.7). 
Building a single-family house range from category A1-A5 as seen in Figure 2.7. According 
to a report from Rambøll [28] comparing numerous buildings in five different European coun-
tries, a GWP impact was found as equation (2.6) shows: 
==PAGE:27==
Literature review 
2.2 Life cycle analysis 
15 
 
 
600.0 kg CO2 eq
m2
 
(2.6) 
 
According to the same report from Rambøll [28], renovation of an existing building ranges 
from category B4-B5 as seen in Figure 2.7 and evaluation of embodied energy found a GWP 
impart as depicted in equation (2.7): 
 
125.0 kg CO2 eq
m2
 
(2.7) 
 
Comparing (2.6) with (2.7) shows that renovating a building result in a reduction of 79% in 
CO2 emissions compared to building new. 
 
Alternatives 
Regarding the aspect of repurposing, it is relevant to involve DGNB about their classification 
schemes and rankings. Repurpose as well as reuse has a positive influence in terms of LCA, 
environmental aspects, and costs. In DGNB terminology, the construction sector must be a 
storage of resources rather than a temporary stage between production and disposal. This pro-
motes the potential of reusing and repurposing which contributes to the conservation of natural 
resources that cannot be regenerated and an overall reduction of generated waste.  
The DGNB Denmark Newsletter [29] emphasizes that in the context of new buildings and 
comprehensive renovations, the focus should be on upgrading the quality level of building ma-
terials instead of categorizing them solely as landfill or for energy utilization, such as burning 
the materials for heating purposes. 
In DGNB terminology, a distinction is made between four quality levels about building mate-
rials after the end of use. These quality levels are divided into QL0 Landfill, QL1 Recycle, 
QL2 Repurpose, and QL3 Reuse. This is also called the waste hierarchy and is shown in Figure 
2.8. 
 
Figure 2.8: Waste hierarchy from the DGNB newsletter [29] 
This criterion calls for a strategy regarding repurposing and reuse with a focus on connections 
between structural components to not reduce the quality of the components. The primary ob-
jective behind this is to facilitate the transition of the component into a new lifecycle.  
 
==PAGE:28==
Literature review 
2.2 Life cycle analysis 
16 
 
Currently, in the DGNB classification, the use of diamond cutting to separating concrete or 
masonry is not recognized as a method for classification as reuse. However, it is classified as 
repurpose seen as QL2 in Figure 2.8, indicating that the method has not demonstrated con-
sistent quality. The newsletter describes that if a market for diamond-cutting separation 
emerges, this classification will be changed [29]. Since the use of hydraulic pumps for separa-
tion is still at the hypothetical stage and not a recognized method, it would require acceptance 
before it could be classified as reuse. The same applies to masonry, which historically consisted 
entirely of lime mortars, where DfD truly comes into play [30]. Masonry constructed with brick 
and lime mortar is easily separable, whereas if the mortar is cement-based, there are significant 
challenges. This is due to the difficulty of separating the mortar from the bricks without dam-
aging them, rendering them non-reusable. The shift towards using cement mortars instead of 
lime mortars is primarily driven by the significant role of time constraints and economics in 
construction projects. Cement mortars achieve higher and much faster strength compared to 
lime mortars, which makes sense from that perspective but not from a design for disassembly 
perspective [31]. 
DGNB rates a project according to its flexibility for future renovations. This is especially rel-
evant in the design phase when buildings change their purpose within their lifetime in terms of 
usage-class, the initial design has a big influence e.g. the number of reinforcements of structural 
elements that need to be made. Typically, the usage class distinguishes between A) domestic 
and residential areas, B) office areas, C) areas where people may congregate, and D) shopping 
areas. The Danish legislation, DS/EN 1991-1-1, regarding imposed loads on structural ele-
ments, differs naturally due to different usage classes.  
All the different categories listed have different characteristic values of actions in terms of the 
magnitude of the loads that need to be used in the design phase.  
This causes some requirements for the conversion from one usage class to another concerning 
structural elements if they need to be reinforced due to different requirements. The same goes 
for requirements in terms of sound and fire requirements, which also change according to usage 
class. The perspective of adopting a flexible construction approach from the beginning, while 
avoiding excessive material expenses and over-dimensioning of structures, entails aligning the 
requirements across various usage classes. This alignment aims to prevent the need for signif-
icant renovations resulting from changes in the designated usage class. 
According to an interview with associate professor Kristoffer Negendahl from DTU, an ongo-
ing project regarding flexibility led by Negendahl was presented. The interview can be found 
in Appendix A.12. The project is collaborated with Henning Larsen Architects, 3XN, and BIG 
and is looking into how to measure the flexibility of buildings. This project dives into the dif-
ferent needs for structural components and how mathematical to scale the flexibility of it. The 
projects take requirements regarding the load, sound, fire, and thermal properties into account 
to investigate whether to over dimensioning the structures without any reinforcement when 
changing the usage class.  
 
==PAGE:29==
Literature review 
2.2 Life cycle analysis 
17 
 
2.2.4 Reuse 
Reuse is when an object or material is used again and again without changing the form of the 
product. As covered in the previous subchapters, there is a need of lowering the CO2 footprint 
for concrete elements. Taking the next step of not only recycling or repurposing the concrete 
is to reuse the concrete elements. A project in Norway was one of the first projects to reuse 
HCSs from an old existing building directly in another building. The project was called KA13, 
the client created a report to evaluate their experiences and results [8]. The report specifically 
showed a reduction of 89% in CO2 emissions when comparing reused HCSs with newly made 
HCSs in stages A1-A4 related to Figure 2.7. 
 
However, estimated annual data from the Ministry of the Environment in Denmark projects an 
even larger reduction in GWP [24]. The report presents the CO2 footprint of the production of 
a European average concrete element in stages A1-A4 as seen in Figure 2.7: 
 
52.0 kg CO2 eq
m2
 
(2.8) 
 
From the Ministry of the Environment in Denmark [24], the footprint from separation and ren-
ovation of the precast concrete structures is assumed to correspond to stages C1-D from Figure 
2.7 resulting in the emission of:  
 
1.8 kg CO2 eq
m2
 
(2.9) 
 
When comparing newly made precast concrete elements (2.8) with reused elements (2.9), the 
largest reduction of CO2 emissions is found as 97% in GWP. 
 
Alternatives 
Reusing the materials will ensure that the materials are not only getting used once but several 
times and thereby moving toward a circular construction phase. If the concrete elements must 
be used more than once, the construction must be designed for that. Design for Disassembly 
(DfD) emerges as a relevant consideration in this context.  
Nowadays, buildings that are designed with prefabricated concrete elements have not yet been 
reused in Denmark. They have only been recycled as crushed concrete in road fill or as aggre-
gates. One of the reasons of only using concrete elements once is that it is difficult to implement 
new principles in the building industry. In implementing DfD in the industry, some principles 
need to be considered [32]:  
1) Comprehensive documentation of materials and methods for deconstruction. 
2) Designing connections and joints which are easy to disassemble, e.g., mechanical 
joints. 
3) Separate mechanical, electrical, and plumbing items. 
==PAGE:30==
Literature review 
2.2 Life cycle analysis 
18 
 
4) More simple construction design. 
5) Design according to labor practices, productivity, and safety.  
Thereby, the construction industry needs to reach a consensus on transitioning towards a more 
circular economy, and approach, even though it may entail initial expenses. At the moment, 
there are no requirements or standards in Denmark on how to reuse concrete HCSs. This must 
be changed if concrete slabs should be reused regularly in construction.   
 
To design for disassembly, the demolition phase must be developed such that the building parts 
can be disassembled easily without damaging them. Connections can be divided into dry 
(purely mechanical), semi-dry (mechanical and concrete/mortar), and wet connections (pure 
concrete/mortar). One way of making disassembly the easiest possible is designing dry con-
nections. This will ensure that the whole element can be used again and no concrete needs to 
be removed before disassembling. Demountable dry connections have been investigated in [33] 
as a review. The review presents that beam/column, and wall/wall mechanical connections 
have been analyzed and reasonable solutions are made. But the study also presents that the 
research regarding beam/beam, beam/slab and slab/slab connections are very limited. But the 
study does present some potential solutions, e.g., the slab/slab connection which can be seen 
in Figure 2.9 below. 
 
 
Figure 2.9: Figure from “Demountable connections of reinforced concrete structures: Review and fu-
ture developments” [33] presents potential mechanical joint in slab/slab connection 
 
As can be seen Figure 2.9, the connection consists of bolts and a steel plate. But as the review 
also states, the embedded steel plate and the bolts through the slab may weaken the concrete 
which will lead to failure in the concrete. This proves that mechanical joints in concrete decks 
are very difficult to design. When designing pure mechanical connections, it is important to 
have in mind that rust will arise over time, which makes it difficult.  
Literature about wet joints in relation to DfD has not been possible to find, besides the studies 
on which this study is based. One study aimed to investigate the shear forces in construction 
joints between concrete and lower-strength mortars. As already stated in this study, using a 
lower-strength mortar as a construction joint will entail cracking in the joint and thereby not 
damaging the concrete decks. Therefore, there is a potential in use of lower-strength mortars 
as long as it succeeds to induce yielding of the reinforcement [9].  
==PAGE:31==
Literature review 
2.2 Life cycle analysis 
19 
 
Another study has investigated the use of hydraulic jacking to separate concrete elements. Fur-
ther information about lower-strength mortars and hydraulic jacking will be covered in future 
subchapters.  
As already described in this section there are challenges in implementing DfD principles in the 
industry. One challenge is that there can be uncertainty regarding the quality of the already-
used materials. Therefore, some documentation must be used, when disassembling the materi-
als [34]. Furthermore, nowadays the demand is low, but when designers begin to implement 
DfD principles, demand will increase, especially if projects using DfD principles are successful 
[32].  
 
Other than the above, there is also a logistic problem that must be considered. When the deck 
elements have been disassembled, they need to be stored and probably examined before being 
transferred to the new building site. The transport must also be taken into consideration when 
calculating LCA as already presented in Figure 2.7. Now, no sites are made for storing already 
used construction elements. Therefore, this must also be implemented. Furthermore, the facil-
ities must fulfill some requirements. For example, dunnage and bracing must be considered, as 
well as the elements must be kept away from soil to prevent staining, cracking, distortion, and 
warping [34].  
 
2.2.5 Comparing global warming potential 
To compare the global warming potential (GWP) of the three different sustainable solutions, 
an iceberg analogy is used to provide a metaphorical insight into the industry's current state, as 
portrayed in Figure 2.10. The illustration presents the GWP calculated for each in Chapters 
2.2.2, 2.2.3, and 2.2.4. 
 
Using an iceberg metaphor, the literature review suggested that the current state of the industry 
is similar to the top of an iceberg. Recycling on the top of the iceberg is the most commonly 
used method at present, as the majority of crushed concrete is already being reused today [24]. 
However, the GWP for recycling is only 18%, while the potential in GWP increases as we look 
below the water surface into more undiscovered solutions. 
==PAGE:32==
Literature review 
2.3 Hydraulics for separating concrete elements 
20 
 
 
Figure 2.10: Iceberg metaphor comparing GWP 
Figure 2.10 shows the potential for reduction in CO2 emissions increases as we descend the 
iceberg. However, the complexity of the methods increases simultaneously, posing a challenge 
for industry stakeholders. The industry is gradually transitioning toward more flexible solu-
tions, which is symbolized by showing repurpose at the water surface. Repurpose is not as 
broadly applied as recycling yet, however, it holds a more significant potential of a 79% reduc-
tion in GWP compared to recycling. 
Lastly, the reuse of HCSs is far from being common practice yet, due to its inherent complexity, 
thus being symbolized below the water surface. Reuse is depicted as a largely undiscovered 
part of the iceberg under the water surface, which holds a huge unused potential that can ensure 
a reduction of 97% reduction in GWP compared to buying new HCSs.  
 
2.3 
Hydraulics for separating concrete elements 
The focus of this master thesis is exploring a DfD-system for grouted HCSs rather than using 
mechanical connections. The reason for this focus is that casting between HCSs is commonly 
used for assembling HCSs in Denmark [35]. Thus, the demand for a DfD method for grouted 
HCSs is much higher.  
 
2.3.1 Hydraulic disassembly 
Hydraulics is commonly used to align and adjust precast concrete elements in intricate struc-
tures. However, the hydraulic pump seen in Figure 2.11 can also serve several other purposes, 
including accurately inducing loads, balancing force loading for multiple sets, and monitoring 
Recycle
Re ur ose
Reuse
   
   
   
==PAGE:33==
Literature review 
2.3 Hydraulics for separating concrete elements 
21 
 
loads during building construction [36]. A product, hydraulic pumps called Flat Jacks, is one 
solution that can be used.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
(a) 
(b) 
Figure 2.11 From Hydra Capsule webpage [36]: 
3D Representation of Flat Jack (a) and jacking system (b) 
Hydraulic pumps as seen in Figure 2.11 (a) are in this thesis used to push the elements apart 
with hydraulic pressure. If the stringer reinforcement tying the HCSs was cut, combined with 
the lower-strength mortar ensured that any cracks would happen in the mortar joint, rather than 
in the elements themselves. To use the hydraulic pumps in the mortar joint, the concrete must 
be milled out, so that the pumps can be placed in the mortar joint. The jacking system can be 
seen in Figure 2.11 (b), the pump and injection cylinder must provide pressure to the flat jack 
that exceeds the shear bearing capacity of the construction joints. In the described thesis, the 
method was successful in conducted, when using three flat jacks, although initial cutting of the 
reinforcement with a diamond saw was necessary [11].  
This report will take it one step further by incorporating DfD principles in the reinforcement 
design into the process aiming to reduce cutting. By interviewing stakeholders in the building 
sector, the report aims to create a comprehensive approach that not only separates precast con-
crete elements without damaging them but also considers implementing DfD. 
 
2.3.2 Lower-strength mortars  
This report examines the strength of lime-cement mortars which are primarily used for masonry 
and a stronger cement-based mortar, which is primarily used for plinths. From tests in the report 
by Cømert [9] the compressive- and flexural strengths of the mortars are carried out according 
to DS/EN 12390, while the determination of the shear strength are determined through testing. 
==PAGE:34==
Literature review 
2.3 Hydraulics for separating concrete elements 
22 
 
To compare results and findings, this study is using identical mortar types, as the report from 
DTU [9]. Therefore, it is assessed to be safe to use relevant strength parameters from the study 
[9]. Hence, this thesis can focus on other test configurations, including the influence of normal 
force and reinforcement. 
The main purpose of using lower-strength mortars is to ensure that the failure will occur in the 
interface and not in the deck elements when e.g., using hydraulic pumps for disassembly. An 
illustration can be seen in Figure 2.12. As the figure illustrates, a shear break in the interface 
between a mortar and the concrete deck will occur. Therefore, the tests performed in this thesis 
are investigating the shear break between a mortar and concrete.  
However, it still requires that the building complies with robustness and fire requirements. 
Using a mortar that is too weak for the joints will weaken the building with the given require-
ments which means that a lower boundary for mortar in the joints is needed to comply with the 
requirements. For that reason, the project is based on three different mortars with different 
strength parameters to determine a lower strength that complies with relevant requirements. 
 
 
Figure 2.12   isassem ly of deck elements usin   ydraulic  um s. Ins ired  y “ esi n For  isas 
sembly of Concrete  lements  sin  Hydraulic Jackin ” [10] 
 
When disassembling the deck elements, it is expected that the very first deck element must be 
disassembled vertically. A simple drawing of that scenario is illustrated in Figure 2.13. This 
vertical disassembly will not be the focus of this thesis, as only shear tests will be carried out.  
==PAGE:35==
Literature review 
2.4 Sub conclusion 
23 
 
 
Figure 2.13: Vertical disassembly of deck element. Inspired by “Design For Disassembly of Concrete 
Elements Using Hydraulic Jacking” [10] 
 
By using the hydraulic pumps strategically to achieving pure shear in the construction joint, it 
is intended to break in the actual adhesion/interface between the concrete deck and the mortar 
or only in the mortar. It would also be possible to spray away the concrete using high-pressure 
water. However, that method has certain weaknesses such as a lot of dirt will arise, and the 
high pressure could damage the HCSs. 
 
In the procedure of disassembling concrete decks with hydraulic pumps, it is particularly im-
portant that unintended bending or torsion does not occur. From the report by Sharaf [11], an 
analysis by using hydraulic pumps to disassemble concrete slabs was made. It was suggested 
that a minimum of three hydraulic pumps are needed to achieve successful cracking in the joint. 
However, these results were entirely achieved using FEM calculations with constant locations. 
Therefore, the results are not unambiguously true, and the use of hydraulic pumps must be 
further investigated with variant positioning and full-scale test [10]. Among other things, it 
must be investigated whether the deck elements are getting damaged when using hydraulic 
pumps.  
 
2.4 
Sub conclusion  
The literature research reviewed multiple circular approaches that have significant potential to 
improve the sustainability of the precast concrete element industry. Repurpose, recycle, and 
reuse were identified as the most meaningful aspects for impact on GWP with reuse being both 
the most promising but also most complex. The findings suggest that industry stakeholders 
should focus on exploring the potential of flexible solutions and reuse to further advance the 
sustainability of precast concrete elements. This thesis aims to contribute to the knowledge and 
understanding of the circular economy in the construction sector and support the industry's 
transition towards a more sustainable future. Finally, background information on lower-
strength mortars and hydraulic pumps was introduced.
 
==PAGE:36==
 
24 
 
This chapter seeks to integrate theory and practices from key stakeholders involved in building 
projects. It is essential to evaluate the current traditional organizational structure of the con-
struction process and comprehend the involvement of various stakeholders in order to shift 
towards circular approaches. 
 
3.1 
Construction organization setup 
A contract setup with a client can vary depending on the client’s wishes [37]. A traditional 
project organization with no regard for specific contract setup is shown below in Figure 3.1.  
 
 
Figure 3.1: Typical stakeholders within a traditional project organization with no regard for contract 
setup based on Anlægsteknik 2 [37]. Number indication in the figure refers to conducted interviews: 
(1) Nordkranen, (2) Rambøll Norge, (3) MOE, (4) NCC Management, (5) Tscherning, (6) Boligbeton, 
(7) Hilti, (8) NCC Production, (9) Social- & Boligstyrelsen, (10) Danish Standard, (11) Danish Techno-
logical Institute, (12) DTU, (13) IDA Conference 
 
Figure 3.1 presents an organizational structure based on the current perspective, the number 
indicated in Figure 3.1 informs which stakeholders have been interviewed as part of this report. 
Chapters 3.2 and 3.3 will elaborate on the outtakes of these interviews, while this chapter de-
scribes the organizational structure that currently exists. 
3 
Input from Stakeholders 
==PAGE:37==
Input from Stakeholders 
3.2 Engaging stakeholders 
25 
 
 
A building project can be initiated from scratch, or it can involve disassembly and recycling of 
construction components from a previous project to create a new final product. The same stake-
holders that are involved in constructing new buildings are also used if a finished building is 
demounted and reused. 
Typically, the client is the driving force behind the building project and serves as the building's 
owner, sometimes in collaboration with a property developer or investor with whom they must 
comply. The client evaluates the potential profitability of the project and then puts it out for 
tender [37].  Architects, engineers, and contractors then participate by submitting proposals for 
the tender, and the client chooses a winner based on factors such as price, quality, and time. 
The engineers and architects must design a solution that meets the client's requirements and 
complies with city legislation, while the contractor must ensure that the building is built cor-
rectly and on schedule [37]. The contractor is often responsible for managing suppliers and 
craftsmen. In Denmark, Social- & Boligstyrrelsen is responsible for city legislation and deter-
mining building regulations by referring to published standards. These standards are developed 
through research-based evidence from research and development (R&D) facilities and univer-
sities (UNI), see Appendix A.9. 
The situation today as conveyed in Figure 3.1 shows that the finished product in a project or-
ganization is determined by the client’s needs and city laws. This master thesis has conducted 
several interviews with key stakeholders as depicted in Figure 3.1 to obtain a comprehensive 
understanding of the subject matter. This is particularly advantageous since the topic being 
investigated is new, and stakeholders with real-life experience can provide valuable insights 
into the challenges, opportunities, and potential solutions related to DfD.  
 
3.2 
Engaging stakeholders 
When it comes to researching new topics and finding solutions, it is essential to gain valuable 
insight that may not be possible through literature and theory alone. It is worth expanding the 
research to include real-life practice. This is accomplished by conducting interviews with stake-
holders who are actively involved in the subject area. Stakeholder interviews provide a valuable 
understanding of challenges, benefits, perspectives, and motivations, helping to identify poten-
tial obstacles and determine the viability of recycling projects for the organization.  
 
Table 3.1 shows which interviews were able to be performed during this master thesis. The 
interviews are based on relevant stakeholders involved in a traditional organization in Figure 
3.1.  
It was key to interview as broadly as possible to comprehend the various aspects in the process, 
as well as get experience from stakeholders who have worked with reused HCSs such as Chris-
tian Gamst from Rambøll Norway who worked as an engineer on KA13, see Appendix A.2.

To engage the stakeholders, they are visually presented in a stakeholder map in Figure 3.2 to 
compare the stakeholders. A stakeholder map is always biased depending on the motivation. 
For this master thesis, the point of view is as an engineer working as part of a partnership for 
green transition in the building sector trying to facilitate DfD-systems in Denmark. The stake-
holder map is based on the 12 conducted interviews as elaborated in  
Table 3.1.  
As background for Figure 3.2 an explanation for the stakeholder’s placement according to in-
terest and influence is elaborated in Appendix B. The map is based on defining the purpose of 
each stakeholder, determining their level of involvement, and identifying their goals. 
==PAGE:39==
Input from Stakeholders 
3.2 Engaging stakeholders 
27 
 
 
Figure 3.2: Stakeholder map with the point of view from an engineer trying to implement DfD 
The number indication in the figure refers to conducted interviews: (1) Nordkranen [Client], (2) 
Rambøll Norge [Engineer], (3) MOE [Engineer], (4) NCC Management [Contractor], (5) Tscherning 
[Contractor], (6) Boligbeton [Supplier], (7) Hilti [Supplier], (8) NCC Production [Craftsmen], (9) Social- 
& Boligstyrelsen [City Legislation], (10) Danish Standard [Publisher], (11) Danish Technological Insti-
tute [R&D], (12) DTU [UNI] 
 
The stakeholder map, as presented in Figure 3.2, provides a visualization of each stakeholder’s 
influence and interest concerning incorporating of a structural DfD-system in the construction. 
See Appendix B for an elaborate description of the stakeholder’s placement on the map. 
The stakeholder map helps to understand each stakeholder relative to the others. When trying 
to establish the reuse of HCSs, the stakeholder map can be used to assess their expectations 
and thus, design strategies to avoid potential conflicts and engage them effectively. Note that 
Figure 3.2 is solely based on the stakeholders interviewed in this report. 
It is seen in Figure 3.2 that suppliers of tools (8) and craftsmen (7) have low interest and influ-
ence, as they are mostly unaffected by the choices because they provide a service and are paid 
accordingly. Contractors (4+5) and publishers (10) are more interested but have low influence 
as they are reliant on other stakeholders, thus they only have to be engaged when needed. Then 
there are the engineers (2+3) and the client (1) who have relatively high influence but are not 
as actively interested. They can be considered passive influencers, as they do not have much 
need themselves, but do have a large impact if wanted. Engineers as well as contractors are 
paid for a job, so they will incorporate DfD if paid for, but not otherwise. However, this is 
solely based on the interviews in this master thesis, of course, different engineers, clients, and 
other stakeholders may have varying priorities and values regarding the green agenda.  
==PAGE:40==
Input from Stakeholders 
3.3 Stakeholder insights 
28 
 
The client is slightly more interested as they experience increasing demands from investors and 
users to be sustainable. However, gaining profit is still the clients’ main priority, they must be 
kept satisfied. Lastly is the manufacturer of HCSs (6) and city legislation (9) which is depend-
ent on innovation from R&D (11) and universities (12). These stakeholders should be managed 
closely as they have an actual high interest and influence on the potential market. They are key 
players in this transition. 
Building upon the insights depicted in Figure 3.1, the stakeholder map reveals three key obser-
vations. Firstly, the client is the initiator and finances the project wielding the highest level of 
influence. However, the client also faces increasing pressure from investors, which impacts 
their decisions toward sustainable solutions. Secondly, contractors can play a persuasive role 
in advocating for more sustainable solutions but are still primarily responsible for executing 
assigned tasks, thus they are dependent on the client’s wishes. Lastly, city legislation, respon-
sible for setting rules and regulations, holds significant influence over the green transition. 
However, they rely heavily on publishers who need innovation from R&D and universities to 
create new practices and ideas. 
These findings highlight the intricate interplay among stakeholders thus underlining the im-
portance of effective engagement and teamwork to successfully implement sustainable prac-
tices.  
 
3.3 
Stakeholder insights 
By comparing the interviews in Table 2.2, it is possible to provide valuable insights and identify 
similarities and differences in the information provided by the different stakeholders. Cross-
referencing the interviews in Table 2.2 helps to verify the accuracy of the information obtained, 
thus accounting for the individual goals and perspectives of each interviewee. 
 
Table 3.2: Similar key findings from each interview 
Key findings 
Interview 
There is a growing customer demand for sustainable initiatives. 
1-7 + 9-12 
Reusing is at the moment more costly compared to building new. 
2, 3, 6 
The incorporation of DfD is vital for success in the circular economy with a 
focus on reuse. A dedicated project for reuse should be also included when 
designing a building. 
2-6 
The absence of disassembly standards and city legislation in Denmark 
poses a challenge. 
2-6, 9-12

difficult paradigm to change.

crucial aspects.

Input from Stakeholders 
3.3 Stakeholder insights 
29 
 
It seems reasonable to avoid combining both demounting of HCS with de-
molishing of the remaining prefab elements on the same floor, as is create 
potential challenges with machinery, worker safety, and overall operational 
efficiency. 
4, 5, 8 
There is a need to develop a searchable database to facilitate efficient or-
dering for clients and enable contractors to plan ahead effectively. 
1+5 
There is a lack of a clear resolution on how and when the client will benefit 
from incorporating. Particularly as the true benefits emerge 50-100 years 
later when the building needs to be disassembled and the HCSs are reused. 
1+12

2, 3 + 6

The key findings that can be concluded from the interviews are drawn from at least two stake-
holders agreeing.  
 
Two more noticeable findings that were only introduced by one stakeholder are, however, still 
worth mentioning. Kristoffer Negendahl from DTU is investigating the possibilities of incor-
porating flexible solutions in concrete structures see Appendix A.12. He is examining how to 
divide the structure into suitable indexes for loads, fire, geometry, etc. that aims to make pre-
fabricated elements easier to reuse for another purpose.  
First mentioned was whether it makes sense to increase the requirements for HCSs used in 
office buildings as they are more likely to transform during a use period of 50 years than a 
housing complex is. Thus, the HCSs in an office building could be able to carry the load of a 
residential building as well. 
Another mention is the challenge of establishing a widely accepted standard for CE marking. 
Various considerations arise when determining the appropriate approach. One option is to 
adopt a comprehensive method that involves numerous destructive tests, offering a high level 
of precision. Alternatively, a less stringent control method could be chosen, prioritizing in-
creased security to minimize the risk of false positives. However, this approach may result in 
more waste.  
One possible approach to address the challenges with quality testing the HCSs could be to 
allow both solutions to coexist. Thus, increasing competition and letting the market self-bal-
ance by evaluating the trade-offs between precision, security, and waste reduction. 
 
 
==PAGE:42==
Input from Stakeholders 
3.4 Sub conclusion 
30 
 
3.4 
Sub conclusion 
Engineers are crucial in making decisions about systems and materials used in construction 
projects, and careful planning and consideration of all factors are essential for project execution 
with a focus on sustainability, safety, and profitability. To scale up the reusing of concrete 
elements, new circular business models, pre-standards, and paradigms for reused building com-
ponents must be established. Furthermore, architects, contractors, and clients emphasize the 
importance of gaining a comprehensive database to search for elements that can be reserved 
and acquired as easily as ordering new elements for integration into projects. 
In summary, the stakeholder map in a project organization is dependent on the client's require-
ments and the laws set by the city legislation. However, the absence of standards for reuse may 
affect the stakeholders' decision-making process, and the report will investigate this further. 
The current situation suggests that financial gain is the primary motivator for stakeholders, 
rather than sustainability or environmental concerns. 
 
==PAGE:43==
 
31 
 
To develop a proper sustainable DfD-system it is deemed necessary to examine the existing 
conditions for HCSs. One thing is how the HCSs should be designed to make them easier to 
separate. However, consideration must also be given to how the HCSs should be designed so 
that they are advantageous to reuse again. This chapter evaluates the current need for HCSs to 
increase reusability by exanimating existing practices and construction techniques such as ge-
ometric designs, acting loads, and fire demands for HCSs. The goal is to provide valuable 
insights into the current designs that can improve future designs, leading to the development 
of more efficient, cost-effective, and sustainable DfD-systems. 
 
4.1 
Data set 
A dataset of over 10,000 HCSs from recent- and ongoing building projects is analyzed. The 
data gathered from NCC’s building sites are only representative of large-scale building pro-
jects. The HCSs are all delivered from the same manufacturer: Boligbeton. Thus, the data can 
be biased by representing three factors: one contractor, one supplier, and only large-scale pro-
jects. All tables and figures in this chapter are made from the collected data if not otherwise 
expressed. 
 
Table 4.1 below provides information regarding the analyzed building sites and how the HCSs 
are distributed. A common characteristic among all these projects is their relatively large scale, 
price-scale and recent year of construction.

4.1 Data set 
32 
 
The chart in Figure 4.1 below shows what the purpose of the rooms on top of the HCSs are 
designed to be. 
 
Figure 4.1: HCSs distributed by room purpose along with average ULS loads 
 
Figure 4.1 shows that 52% of the HCS in the data are designed to carry apartment floors, 7.4% 
for office floors, and 17.6% for parking spaces. Another 7.5% is made up for the roofs. Lastly, 
15.5% are designed to carry green areas. This indicates that the HCSs are mainly indicative of 
the design of apartment buildings. The average ULS loads in Figure 4.1 are increasing along 
the x-axis. It can be seen that approximately 65% of the HCSs have ULS loads below 10 kN/m2. 
This means that a large portion of the HCSs is limited from being reused for other purposes 
than apartments. However, it also shows that all of the HCSs can be reused in apartment com-
plexes again.  
 
Thus, it could be an option to increase the capacity of all HCSs to a common critical design 
load so that all HCSs can be reused in any situation. However, this needs thorough calculations 
to assess whether it is more or less sustainable to increase the amount of the material used in 
order to improve reusability or vice versa. This lies within the scope of the flexibility arrange-
ment to determine and not within this master thesis. 
Though it must also be noted that the ULS loads are not the only factor influencing the final 
design. Other factors such as SLS loads, fire resistance, length span, and recesses can be as 
critical a factor as the ULS loads. This underlines the complexity of allocating reused HCSs in 
new building projects as each HCS must be individually assessed according to all aspects of 
the HCS design.  
 
The data displayed in Figure 4.1 presents an opportunity to compare the HCS data with the 
progression of building types in the whole of Denmark, as depicted in Figure 4.2 using statis-
tical records from Statistics Denmark [39]. The objective of this comparative analysis is to gain 
==PAGE:45==
Empirical analysis of recently mounted HCSs 
4.1 Data set 
33 
 
valuable insights into the changing dynamics and demands of the building sector. By doing so, 
it aims to identify potential design opportunities for DfD-systems. 
 
Figure 4.2: Completed buildings areas each year according to Statistics Denmark BYGV88 [39]  
 
Figure 4.2 depicts a period of the last 25 years from 1998 in Denmark. Residential and com-
mercial buildings have varied over time while construction of other buildings has stayed rela-
tively stable. When comparing the total change from the past 25 years, it is evident that com-
mercial buildings have gone from dominating the market in 1998 to have declined by around 
1/3 towards 2022. On the contrary, it is seen that residential buildings experienced an increase 
of 4/5 with a steadily rising curve from 2015. Thus, the two building types have changed the 
market lead from commercial- to residential buildings. The current situation shows that 50% 
being built is residential, 31% is commercial and the remaining 19% is for other types of build-
ings. It could be an indication that fewer and fewer stores are necessary due to online sales, etc. 
However, this can quickly change in the future with other business models that we cannot 
predict today. 
 
This growth in the construction of residential buildings could be an answer to the previous 
remark from Figure 4.1 to increase the strength of the HCSs to fit all types of buildings. The 
market is everchanging, thus opting for incorporating flexibility in the DfD-system seems rea-
sonable as presented in Appendix A.12. Statistics Denmark indicates for the moment that res-
idential buildings will keep dominating the market. The common design ground for HCSs 
could for instance be a min ULS load of 10 kN/m2, thus all reused HCSs would fit at least 50% 
of the current market.

4.2 Design of ULS load capacities

4.2 
Design of ULS load capacities 
In continuation of analyzing acting loads and room purposes, Figure 4.3 is representative of 
HCSs decks thickness compared to a defined index of average ULS loads. 
 
 
Figure 4.3: ULS loads and deck thickness compared 
 
In Figure 4.3, the ULS loads rise as the thickness increases of the HCS. However, it is seen that 
one thickness can carry multiple categories of ULS loads. This underlines the complexity of 
the design, as many other factors are also involved such as span length, number of reinforce-
ment wires, and fire requirements, thus you can’t just take a 320 mm HCS from a project and 
reuse it directly in another building project. Figure 4.3 supports the argument from stakeholder 
findings in Table 3.2, that a DfD-system must include a project for reuse to support DfD-sys-
tems.

of the tendencies.

Table 4.2 shows that the area of wires in HCSs somewhat increases with the thickness, except 
the HCSs with a thickness of 400 mm. They have a small area of wires compared to smaller 
thicknesses. The distribution of wires in the HCSs underlines that a more standardized use of 
wires is required. Spaencom, a large manufacturer of HCSs in Denmark is already in the pro-
cess of evolving software to optimize the standardization of wires used in HCSs [40].  
 
Note that SLS loads can have a critical aspect in the design of HCSs as well, however, they 
were not possible to gain access to. 
 
4.3 
Fire resistance 
When designing HCSs an essential aspect that needs to be addressed in the building design 
requirements is fire resistance. A graphical representation of the different fire resistance re-
quirements can be observed in Figure 4.4. 
==PAGE:48==
Empirical analysis of recently mounted HCSs 
4.3 Fire resistance 
36 
 
 
Figure 4.4: Fire resistance requirements 
Figure 4.4 illustrates the share of fire resistance requirements, it reveals that 85% of the HCS 
designs are engineered to withstand a 120-minute fire, while the remaining 15% are designed 
for a 60-minute fire resistance. This distribution of fire resistance aligns appropriately with 
how the specific room purposes are in the data set as indicated in Figure 4.1. 
 
However, it must be noted that the fire resistance stated in Figure 4.4 is based on the require-
ments for the specific room purpose. In reality, there are challenges for HCSs to withstand a 
fire for such a long duration. Therefore, it should be noted that suppliers declare that HCSs can 
only meet fire regulations if they are further reinforced per 1.2 m as declared in EN 1168.  
According to Hertz [41], no calculations or tests are supporting a fire resistance of extruded 
HCSs for 120 minutes. Tests and analyses suggest that there is evidence of fire resistance of 
approximately 25 minutes. Figure 4.5 below shows a HCS exposed to a 25-minute fire. 
 
 
Figure 4.5: Photo by K. Hertz [35]: 
Damages on HCSs after 25 minutes of exposed fire  
Figure 4.5 shows that the unreinforced bottom flange is damaged. This implies that the cross-
section, according to concrete code DS/EN 1992-1-2, should be considered reduced to a ribbed 
cross-section because the bottom flange cannot be accounted for after 25 minutes of exposed 
==PAGE:49==
Empirical analysis of recently mounted HCSs 
4.4 Geometric comparisons 
37 
 
fire. This indication aligns with the principle of DS/EN 1992-1-2, which states that the possi-
bility of detachment of the bottom concrete should be considered. 
This is an important detail to remember when designing the building and considering the reuse 
of HCSs. Alternatively, they can be fire-protected with insulation, avoiding the need for de-
signing fire reinforcement as indicated in Chapter 2.1.2. Fire insulation of HCSs is however 
not widely adopted due to the additional cost and potential architectural impact. 
Implementing fire insulation instead would address the issue of fire reinforcement requirements 
stated in Chapter 2.1.2, which mandates fire reinforcement every 1.2 meters. This emphasizes 
that HCSs should be fire-protected with insulation regardless of the fire resistance require-
ments. 
Referring back to Figure 4.4, highlights the fact that a significant proportion of the HCSs are 
designed to meet the highest fire resistance requirements has positive implications for their 
reusability, as they can fit any purpose, while 15% of HCSs can only be used for instances of 
60-minute fires.  
 
4.4 
Geometric comparisons 
Moving on from design loads to geometric design, the following figures are representative of 
span lengths, widths, amount of hammerheads, and recesses. 
A distribution of thickness in the data set can be seen below in Figure 4.6. 
 
Figure 4.6: Distribution of thickness 
Figure 4.6 shows that 180 mm thickness only constitutes 1% of all the HCSs in the data set, 
while 220 mm HCS constitutes 44% with an increasing frequency as the thickness increases. 
This indicates that conclusions regarding 180 mm HCSs do not have enough data to support an 
appropriate tendency. 
==PAGE:50==
Empirical analysis of recently mounted HCSs 
4.4 Geometric comparisons 
38 
 
Figure 4.7 shows the distribution of length. 
 
 
(a) 
 
(b) 
Figure 4.7: Length distribution as a column chart (a) and a box plot (b) with min and max = data 
range, x = mean, o = outliers, Q1 = lower 25%, Q2 = 50%, Q3 = upper 75% 
 
It can be seen in Figure 4.7 (a) that the distribution of lengths is relatively similar to a normal 
distribution curve thus indicating that the lengths of HCSs are representative of HCSs in apart-
ment complexes. The chart in Figure 4.7 (a) shows a few deviations from the normal distribu-
tion, whereas some individual long spans above the geometric average are seen to have a much 
higher frequency than the geometric average lengths. This can be due to a large scale of repet-
itive HCSs in the building project designs or indicating that large spans are desired. Figure 4.7 
(b) shows a box plot to visualize the distribution, which shows the ‘whiskers’ to indicate the 
range of that data set with max and min values indicated, with a few outliers at the top. The 
box plot indicates the lower and upper quarter Q1 and Q3 respectively, which indicates 25% 
of the data lies in between the bottom whisker and Q1, while 75% lies between the bottom 
whisker and Q3. The median is shown as Q2 being 5.6 m. The box plot shows that 50% of the 
HCSs lie between the length of 4 to 6.8 meters. Thus, it could make sense to aim for a larger 
span of HCSs in the DfD-system, as HCSs below 4 meters are unable to fit the purpose in 75% 
of how we use HCSs today.

Figure 4.8: Width distribution as a column chart (a) and a box plot (b) with min and max = data 
range, x = mean, o = outliers, Q1 = lower 25%, Q2 = 50%, Q3 = upper 75% 
 
As seen in Figure 4.8 (a), the widths of HCSs show much more indication of standardization 
than the span lengths as the exact widths of 1.2 and 2.4 meters are highly represented. This is 
from the fact that 1.2 and 2.4 meters are standardized at the suppliers to increase effectiveness 
and lower production costs [35]. Figure 4.8 (b) shows that Q3 and max are both at 2.4 meters. 
The median Q2 is 2.1 m and from Q1 it is seen that 75% of the HCSs are wider than 1,2 meters 
while in Figure 4.8 (a) it can be seen that 2.4 meters are twice as frequent as 1.2 meters. This 
is most likely because 2.4 meters wide HCSs will half the number of lifts needed by a crane to 
mount an equal area with 1.2 meters wide HCSs. Thus, using 2.4 meters wide HCSs a contrac-
tor can reduce the total time of production on the building site and lower building expenses for 
cranes.

of 5.6 m from Figure 4.9 (b). 220 and 320 mm HCSs from Figure 4.9 (b) and (d) are distributed 
more evenly along the median with some peaks both above and below the median being more 
frequent. 270 mm HCSs Figure 4.9 (c) in seen tilting towards longer spans. Figure 4.9 (e) shows
