metric middle lengths. 400 mm HCSs have a peak close to 70% of load index 21-30 kN/m2 
being at around five meters lengths and load index of 11-20 kN/m2 peaks at 6.5 meters for 45%. 
400 mm HCSs tend to span shorter than 270 and 320 mm slabs, which can be an explanation 
of why the amount of wires in Table 4.2 does not increase as the thickness increases. The span 
length and applied loads are both critical factors for calculating the requirements of the HCSs. 
 
4.5 
Adaptions 
Standardization of HCSs has greatly evolved throughout the years but building requirements 
have also increased. It has become standard practice to incorporate hammerheads in the perim-
eter stringers to obtain shear stresses in the floor diaphragm. Hammerheads are a well-estab-
lished solution, but the recesses made in the HCSs pose a challenge when trying to reuse the 
elements. The recesses must either be fitted perfectly in another project when reused or the 
elements must have the recesses grouted. Figure 4.10 below shows the distribution of hammer-
heads. 
 
Figure 4.10: Hammerheads frequency 
It can be seen in Figure 4.10 that half of the HCSs have at least one hammerhead. 9.2% have 
precisely one hammerhead, 23.3% have two, 5.7% have three and the remaining 11.2% have 
between four and twelve hammerheads.  
It is remarkable that half of the HCSs do have hammerheads when they are only needed at the 
sides of HCSs in the perimeter. Figure 4.10 depicts that two hammerheads are more likely to 
be used than one, and the trendline indicates an exponential decline in the frequency of the 
number of hammerheads. 
==PAGE:54==
Empirical analysis of recently mounted HCSs 
4.5 Adaptions 
42 
 
 
Following Figure 4.10 is Figure 4.11, which gives the total amount of adaptions that are made 
in HCSs. An adaptation is either a hammerhead, recess or sides that have been cut to fit the 
project. 
 
 
Figure 4.11: HCS with/without adaptions 
Figure 4.11 shows that 78% of HCS have a type of adaption made to fit a project thus making 
the unique and evading standard designs. These adaptions pose a challenge because the 78% 
of HCSs for reuse will either have recesses and cut sides that must be cast with concrete or fit 
directly in another project or they have hammerhead recesses that have been grouted with con-
crete and need to be removed before the HCS is allowable to be reused. 
 
An effective solution to avoid the need for cleaning grout from hammerheads when they are to 
be reused, is to implement mechanical connections. One particularly suitable approach is to 
use flat steel plates positioned on top of the HCSs after they have been grouted. These flat steel 
plates are drilled onto the slabs, securely holding them together as seen in Figure 4.12. This 
method is already commonly employed as a solution when issues arise during the casting pro-
cess.  
 
Figure 4.12: Photo by Anders Rafn: Flat steel plates used in a project 
While mechanical connections offer advantages, it's important to consider potential drawbacks. 
One concern is that mechanical connections can struggle to reach the same level of strength as 
reinforced concrete. Additionally, these connections require to be protected against corrosion, 
==PAGE:55==
Empirical analysis of recently mounted HCSs 
4.6 Sub conclusion 
43 
 
requiring additional measures and maintenance. Furthermore, implementing mechanical con-
nections introduces an extra step in the construction process, thus increasing construction ex-
penses. As a result, a solution for cast concrete should be considered instead. 
 
Another solution to mitigate the number of hammerheads required in a project while casting is 
to standardize their placement and increase their strength. By implementing consistent guide-
lines for the placement of hammerheads, such as always positioning them 1200 mm from the 
ends, they can be designed to connect with other elements in different projects. Additionally, 
modifying the design of hammerheads can contribute to reducing their quantity. Typically, 
hammerheads are designed with a diameter of Ø10. However, increasing the diameter to Ø14 
would increase the strength per hammerhead and thus reduce the overall amount. 
 
4.6 
Sub conclusion 
The analysis of the HCS data in this chapter highlights their complexity and the various factors 
that influence their design. ULS loads play a significant role, but it is crucial to consider other 
critical factors such as SLS loads, fire resistance, span length, and recesses. This complexity 
underlines the need for individual assessment of each HCS based on multiple design aspects 
when allocating them for reuse in new building projects. It also builds upon the need for a 
database as mentioned in Chapter 3.3, acquiring reused elements should be as effortless as 
ordering new elements for seamless integration into projects. 
From this chapter it can be concluded that to enhance the reusability of HCSs and promote 
sustainable DfD-systems, standardization is essential. The conclusion from this chapter builds 
upon the idea of flexibility introduced in Chapter 3.3. By categorizing HCSs into indexes based 
on certain criteria, such as load bearing capacity, fire resistance and other relevant factors, it 
would become easier to assess their potential for reuse in future projects. This categorization 
system could provide a standardized framework for evaluating the suitability of HCSs, enabling 
architects and engineers to identify larger groups of elements that can be effectively reused in 
future projects. 
Incorporating guidelines that establish fixed indexes for calculating different types of slabs can 
help limit the variability in ULS loads and wire configurations. Additionally, considering the 
possibility of designing slabs to carry higher loads than necessary can facilitate their adaptation 
in future projects. 
 
 
==PAGE:56==
 
  
44 
 
This chapter is evaluating the gained knowledge to give a general and specific proposal on how 
to incorporate a DfD-system that reduced cutting as much as possible and increased effective-
ness. It aims to provide a simple solution that tries to address the majority of the identified 
issues as well as being able to be further tested in Chapter 6.  
 
5.1 
Comparative evaluation 
This section provides a brief summary of the knowledge gained from previous chapters and 
compares them as common ground for the DfD-system. 
 
Literature review 
In the literature review conducted for this master thesis, several important insights were ob-
tained. HCSs have challenges in meeting fire safety regulations on their own. To ensure the 
construction's safety, strict reinforcement requirements against fire have been introduced. 
These requirements are determined through empirical data tests and simply cannot be easily 
altered. 
The procedure for CE-marking of HCSs has not yet been published in Denmark. However, it 
is currently under development, and researchers are exploring non-destructive test methods. 
Once the CE-marking is released, it is anticipated to share similarities with the Norwegian-
published Eurocode NS 3682:2022 [13]. This will most likely involve a requirement to clean 
the elements from any excess grout as described in the Norwegian standard.  
The use of low mortar strength concrete is gaining attention and undergoing promising devel-
opments for potential applications of using concrete with lower-strength characteristics. The 
literature review also provides evidence that hydraulic disassembly of HCSs is indeed possible.  
 
Input from stakeholders 
During the interviews of stakeholders, the engineers and manufactures emphasize the difficulty 
of changing fire reinforcement requirements as seen in Appendix A.2, Appendix A.3 and Ap-
pendix A.6 Appendix. They also urge to focus on utilizing existing standard details, such as 
D1-D3 from Figure 2.4 and Figure 2.5, as they will always be present when using HCSs. They 
also highlight the importance of including a project plan for reuse to achieve successful out-
comes.
5 
Design for disassembly 
==PAGE:57==
Design for disassembly 
5.2 General proposals 
45 
 
Contractors deem the use of hydraulic disassembly as possible, but it is advisable to avoid 
stringer reinforcement inside HCSs to prevent the need for cutting. To disassemble a building, 
including all prefabricated elements, the demounting processes will include demounting of all 
the construction elements as it is highly expensive and complex to both disassemble and de-
mount at the same time, which is further elaborated in Appendix A.4, Appendix A.5 and Ap-
pendix A.8. Incorporating a solution for lifting gear within the elements and providing recess 
areas for hydraulic flat jacks are also deemed highly beneficial. 
Mechanical connections are effective for disassembly, but they are undesirable due to their 
complexity in design, accessibility requirements, and potential issues with strain and rust. 
High-pressure water jets are also a potential method for separating existing grout, although 
their success is not deemed as likely as presented in Appendix A.4 and Appendix A.8. 
Architects, contractors, and clients express the need for a comprehensive database of reused 
elements that can be reserved and purchased for integration into new designs. They also men-
tion a lack of understanding regarding how to be rewarded for incorporating a DfD-system, 
could it be through certificates or CO2 reduction in the project. Clear regulations are necessary 
to establish legal responsibilities for implementing a DfD-system in a structure intended to 
remain intact as found in Appendix A.1, Appendix A.2, Appendix A.3 and Appendix A.6. 
 
Data analysis of HCS 
The data revealed the standardization of HCSs has greatly evolved, but project-specific adap-
tations affect the majority of HCS designs which present challenges for the reuse of the ele-
ments. The data analysis showed that most of the HCSs are unique and must be assessed indi-
vidually. This could be mitigated by establishing fixed indexes for ULS loads and wire designs, 
which would enhance flexibility but also require additional materials.  
Furthermore, there is a need to explore alternative solutions to hammerheads that do not require 
cutting, as hammerheads are the only detail where cutting cannot be avoided. It was found that 
at least 50% of all HCSs contain at least one hammerhead. 
 
5.2 
General proposals 
This chapter provides three proposals as a general suggestion on how to implement a DfD-
system based on the evaluation of the previous chapters as given in section 5.1. 
 
This thesis recommends that the following proposals should be implemented in any certifica-
tion system that serves to promote green transitioning, which for instance could be the DGNB 
certification [42]. The proposals are not rules, as they are only á solution and more proposals 
should be provided to increase the implementation of DfD. The proposals are specifically not 
suggested as being rules, as certain projects are exposed to extensive loads, that require solu-
tions that differ from the given proposal. This allows for a project to implement the proposals 
on the majority of the project while some floors are allowed to diverge.  
==PAGE:58==
Design for disassembly 
5.3 Specific suggestions 
46 
 
First proposal 
“Every construction project should include a project for reuse.” 
It is clearly necessary to extend project designs to include a project for reuse if this has to 
succeed as proposal one constitutes. The project for reuse must be established to ensure clarity 
on which HCSs are worth reusing and if they are adaptable. This can also include an index for 
ULS loads on the slabs and a database to survey reusable HCSs. 
 
Second proposal 
“Should use lower-strength mortars for grouting between HCSs.” 
This second proposal will ensure possibilities for using hydraulics for separation, as the lower-
strength mortar will control the forming of cracks in the concrete under stress, while still being 
able to withstand ULS loads. The use of lower-strength mortar will also create possible alter-
natives to hydraulics such as the use of high-pressure water jets. Establishing a standard for 
specific values of strength required for the mortars in different designs needs further investiga-
tion including full-scale testing. 
 
Third proposal 
“Reinforcement stringers should be avoided inside of the hollow cores in the HCS.” 
The third proposal is deemed necessary for two major reasons. Firstly, it is only possible to 
push HCS horizontally from each other using hydraulic without cutting reinforcement if there 
is only reinforcement around the perimeter of the slabs. Secondly, in Norway, the standard, NS 
3682:2022, has determined as part of the CE-marking that all grouting must be removed from 
reused HSCs, which is also expected to apply in a future Danish version as well. 
 
5.3 
Specific suggestions 
To make the DfD-system feasible, the general third proposal is elaborated for the three most 
expected standard details D1-D3 as described in section 2.1.2. 
 
5.3.1 Specific DfD design suggestions for D1 and D2 
The detail drawing for D1 and D2 is unchanged for the HCSs that are 1.2 meters wide, but 
the reinforcement diameter and frequency is changed for slabs with widths above 1.2 meters. 
==PAGE:59==
Design for disassembly 
5.3 Specific suggestions 
47 
 
 
(a) 
 
(b) 
Figure 5.1: inspired by DS/EN 1992-1-1: 
DfD proposal for standard element widths for HCS 1.2 m (a) and 2.4 m (b) 
DS/EN 1992-1-1 elaborates requirements for reinforcement regarding robustness and fire, that 
it is necessary to have a longitudinal rebar every 1.2 meters to tie with peripheral or internal 
stringer reinforcement. For the 1.2 m wide slabs, the rebars can lie between the slabs as seen 
in Figure 5.1 (a), however for a 2.4 m wide slab, a rebar in the middle of the slab is needed 
inside of a hollow core Figure 5.1 (b).  
The general solution is to avoid rebars inside the hollow cores in a HCS. For a 2.4 meter wide 
HCS it is done by increasing the distance from 1.2 meter to 2.4 meter and compensating by 
increasing the reinforcement diameter for the rebars. It indicated in Figure 5.1 (a) and (b) by a 
green ✓ for optimal placement of reinforcement and a red x for reinforcement to be eliminated. 
Thus, all rebars are only next to the HCSs and thus able to be pushed horizontally from each 
other without the need to cut reinforcement. 
By fire-insulating HCSs, the need for additional fire-reinforcement in D1 and D2, can be elim-
inated. It would be recommended to insulate either way because exposure to fire weakens the 
concrete and causes spalling, compromising the structural integrity of the HCSs. Nevertheless, 
in this thesis, it is assumed that insulating HCSs is not a preferred solution. Therefore, efforts 
are focused on enhancing the current design for fire-reinforcement effectively. 
A simple calculation to ensure a proper fire-reinforcement area following DS/EN 1992-1-1 is 
shown in equations (5.1) and (5.2). Original reinforcement area per 2.4 m slab with rebars every

5.4 
Sub conclusion 
To conclude this chapter, a general and specific solution which is based on the literature review, 
stakeholder interviews, and analysis of recently mounted HCSs was found to be feasible. The 
proposals are simple yet address the problem. The core finding is that HCSs can be separated 
using hydraulics as long as the second and third proposals are realized. Implementation of the 
DfD-system should perhaps start in the certification programs while further investigation on 
how to be implemented in Eurocodes, CE marking, DoP, DS standards, etc. continues. 
The DfD-system presented in this chapter provides an in-depth investigation through practical 
tests, as further explored in the subsequent Chapter 6.
 
==PAGE:63==
 
 
  
51 
 
As presented in the literature review in Chapter 2.3, the main purpose of this thesis is to inves-
tigate separation of HCSs using hydraulics while still ensuring the integrity of the elements.  
Separating HCSs without damaging the elements requires that the crack formation and separa-
tion occur in the interface between the grout and the deck element or directly in the grout. One 
solution to approach this problem is to use a lower-strength mortar as grout, surrounding the 
deck elements, as presented in Chapter 2.3.  
This chapter will investigate the connection between the HCS and the grout by performing 
numerous low-scale shear tests with 18 different configurations. Prior to this thesis, similar 
tests have been carried out with similar mortars and concrete prescriptions [9]. However, the 
test portfolio in this research is expanded in terms of numerous configurations. The configura-
tions various between mortar type, degree of reinforcement as well as with and without normal 
force. This is further elaborated and described in Chapters 6.3, 6.4, and 6.5. 
During the tests, three different mortars with varying strengths were used. The three different 
mortars are respectively Weber C100/400 [43], Weber KC50/50/750 [44], and Weber 
KC35/65/650 [45]. A detailed description of the mortars is presented in Chapter 6.3.3. 
 
The test specimens were cast to replicate the detail drawings D1 and D2 in Figure 2.4. One half 
consists of concrete, imitating a concrete deck element, and the other half consists of a lower-
strength mortar, imitating the grout between the deck elements. 
Figure 6.1 below shows a picture of the test setup.

Test of shear connections 
6.1 Modified Mohr-Coulomb failure criterion 
53 
 
6.1 
Modified Mohr-Coulomb failure criterion 
One of the main focuses of the thesis is testing shear connections of specimens consisting of 
concrete and different mortar types cast together. In relation to shear transfer, it is relevant to 
introduce the modified Mohr-Coulomb failure criterion which is based on cohesion and internal 
friction [46]. The failure criterion distinguishes between sliding failure and separation failure. 
Sliding failure occurs cf. the Coulomb frictional hypothesis when shear stress, τ, exceeds the 
sliding resistance. This is illustrated in Figure 6.3. The contribution to the sliding resistance is 
based on the cohesion and internal friction in the interface related to normal stress.  
 
 
Figure 6.3: Modified Mohr Coulomb failure criterion [46] 
 
The modified Mohr-Coulomb failure criterion is defined as: 
 
|τ| = c ± μ ⋅ σ 
(6.1) 
Equation (6.1) describes the relation between the shear stress, τ, and the normal stress, σ. The 
positive constants c and µ are the cohesion and friction coefficients. The cohesion is related to 
the shear strength of the material while the friction coefficient is based on the roughness of the 
surface in the interface of the joint between the two materials. The friction coefficient is related 
to the internal angle of friction, φ, and is defined as µ = tan(φ).  
It is important to state that the contribution from normal stress depends on whether it is com-
pressive or tensile stress. Compressive stress entails a positive contribution whereas tensile 
stress entails a negative contribution.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:66==
Test of shear connections 
6.1 Modified Mohr-Coulomb failure criterion 
54 
 
The separation failure occurs when the tensile stress exceeds the separation resistance defined 
as the tensile strength of the material: 
 
σ = ft 
(6.2) 
 
The conditions for sliding and separation failure are transformed into relations between the 
principal stresses, σ1, and σ3: 
 
(σ1 - σ3)
2
 = c cosφ −
(σ1 + σ3)
2
 sinφ 
(6.3) 
The theory from the modified Mohr-Coulomb failure criterion is an approximate yet not a per-
fect way to describe the yield criterion of concrete. However, the modified Mohr-Coulomb 
failure criterion from equation (6.1) does not take the contribution of reinforcement into ac-
count. Thus, the criterion is adjusted by introducing the contribution from the steel into DS/EN 
1992-1-1. 
The shear stress between two concrete castings is from DS/EN 1992-1-1 expressed as: 
 
vRdi = cfctd + μσnd + ρfyd (μ sinα + cosα)  ≤  0.5ννfcd 
(6.4)

to α = 90° which reduces equation (6.4) to

(6.5)

the tested shear capacities.

6.2 
Grashof’s formula  
To convert a shear force to shear stress, Grashof’s formula is used. This calculation is relevant 
as the shear forces obtained from tests must be converted to shear stresses so that the compar-
ison using equation (6.5) is possible in Chapter 6.6.6. Thus, the formula will be presented in 
this chapter. 
The shear stress along a rectangular cross-section is determined in the following way cf. 
Grashof’s formula: 
 
τ  = Q ΔS
b Iy
 
(6.6) 
The shear stress distribution, τ, is along the cross-section depending on the shear force, Q, the 
first moment of area, ΔS, the width of the cross-section, b, and lastly the moment of inertia, Iy.

height h1 is given as:  
 
ΔS1 = bh1 (h

6.3.2 Compressive strength test of concrete  
During the first casting, eight additional concrete cylinders were cast to determine the com-
pressive strength of the concrete as a control. The cylinders were cast, stored, and tested ac-
cording to DS/EN 12390-1:2021 [47], DS/EN 12390-2:2019 [48], and DS/EN 12390-3:2019 
[49].  
After 31 days of hardening, the cylinders were geometrically measured and tested to failure. 
Optimally, the cylinders were tested after 28 days, but due to technical problems with the test 
machine, the cylinders were tested after 31 days. As the concrete strength is developing slowly 
after 28 days, it is estimated that the three days of difference do not have a significant influence 
on the 28-days strength.

6.3.3 Mortar types  
As mentioned in the introduction, three different types of mortars were used in the tests. Two 
of which are lime and cement based, and one cement based. The two lime-based mortars are a 
combination of cement and lime and have their primary use as joints in masonry constructions. 
The cement-based mortar is primarily used as a cement plaster for repairing robust construction 
parts such as plinths.  
 
The three different mortars were respectively Weber C100/400, Weber KC50/50/750, and We-
ber KC35/65/650. Table 6.5 presents an overview of the mortars. The lime-cement ratio, com-
pressive strength, flexural strength, shear strength, and water addition are listed in Table 6.5. 
The compressive flexural and shear strength of the mortars have not been tested in this study, 
thus values from Cømert [9] have been used.

As Table 6.5 illustrates, there is a huge difference in compressive strength whether the mortar 
is pure cement based or a mixture of lime and cement.  
The presence of lime in the mortar does change the hardening process compared to a cement-
based mortar. Generally, it can be said that the hardening process extends with the presence of 
lime [50].  
For lime-based mortars, it applies that the hardening process of lime needs air to dry out and 
when the water content decreases to approximately 7%, the carbonatation activates with the 
presence of carbon dioxide [50]. Carbonatation starts when calcium hydroxide reacts with car-
bon dioxide and the reaction product is calcium carbonate and water, as illustrated in equation 
(6.11) below:  
 
Ca(OH)2 + CO2 → CaCO3 + H2O 
(6.11) 
 
The hardening process of the cement in the mortar will occur with the presence of water which 
is also known as hydration. The strength development for cement is significantly faster than 
for lime. For cement-based mortars, it can occur that the hardening process will stop due to 
lack of water and the mortar will potentially dehydrate. By combining cement and lime, the 
hardening process will proceed simultaneously but at different rates meaning that hydration 
proceeds faster than carbonatation. The benefit of a combination is that cement remains hy-
drated by absorbing the water released from carbonatation [50]. 
Mentioning carbonation in concrete, the carbonation ingress is worth considering. The carbon-
ation ingress starts from the outside of the concrete and moves toward the inside. 
It is possible to predict the carbonation depth in concrete, knowing the strength of the concrete 
and the time after concrete casting. The formula is expressed as follows, assuming that the 
relative humidity is 50% [51]. 
 
xCaCO3 = 72 ( 1
√fc
 - 0.126) √𝑡 
(6.12) 
where 
xCaCO3 
is the carbonation depth in [mm] 
fc  
is the compressive strength of the concrete in [MPa] 
t 
is the time in [yr] 
 
==PAGE:73==
Test of shear connections 
6.3 Materials and design 
61 
 
The equation is shown in Figure 6.7. As illustrated in the adjacent plot, a relative humidity 
ranging from 40% to 70% exhibits the maximum influence on carbonation depth. Furthermore, 
the graph demonstrates that carbonation depth increases in proportion to the concrete's lower 
strength. Consequently, using lower-strength mortars as construction joints will contribute to 
a greater carbonation depth.  
 
Figure 6.7: Carbonation ingress in concrete. Modified from Betonhåndbogen, chap. 19.2 [51] 
An increased carbonation depth can lead to a reduction in concrete strength over time. Conse-
quently, this may be considered advantageous in relation to the DfD as that will ensure cracks 
in the lower-strength mortar.

6.4.1 Casting process  
The casting process lasted for two days. On the initial day, the concrete was proportioned, 
mixed, and cast in the molds. On the second day, the mortars were cast in the same molds as 
the concrete. 
For the concrete mix, an automatic mixer was used, where the aggregates were weighted and 
added automatically. When the concrete was mixed, it was cast into the molds. The slump and 
air content were measured in every concrete mix, which is also described in Chapter 6.3.1 and 
stated in Table 6.3. Pictures of the concrete cast can be seen in Figure 6.9. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The molds were placed as seen in Figure 6.9 (a) and filled with concrete to the edge. As the 
picture also shows, the side plate was mounted with a clamp. To minimize the air volume in 
the concrete, the molds with fresh concrete were vibrated on a vibration table for 30 to 60 
seconds. After casting, the molds were covered with plastic. The day after casting, the side 
plate was removed as Figure 6.9 (b) illustrates. Thereby, the different mortars were mixed and 
cast into the molds.  
Only water was added to the cement when mixing the mortars. The amount of water added for 
the different mortars can be seen in Table 6.5. The mixing was performed in a forced action 
mixer and was carried out according to the instructions on the mortar declaration, which can 
be found in Appendix D. Figure 6.10 shows the casting process for the mortars. 
Figure 6.9: (a) Molds standing upside down. (b) Molds with concrete on day 2 
(a) 
(b) 
==PAGE:76==
Test of shear connections 
6.4 Test preparation 
64 
 
 
The mortars were mixed and cast into molds in the empty half as can be seen in Figure 6.10 
(a). When grouting concrete between HCSs, self-consolidating concrete (SCC) is normally 
used. It is highly favorable as the concrete is easier to work with and will fill the formwork to 
completion and encapsulate the reinforcement without any need to vibrate [52]. The mortars 
were vibrated and leveled as illustrated in Figure 6.10 (b).  
After the casting of the mortars was completed, the molds were covered with plastic. Four days 
after casting, the elements were separated from the molds (again covered with plastic) so that 
new elements could be cast in the molds.  
During the 28 days of hardening, the specimens were stored in the casting hall. The temperature 
and relative humidity (RH) were not measured doing the hardening. But experientially, it is 
assumed that the temperature was around 22℃. As the specimens were covered with plastic, it 
is assumed that RH was 80% - 90%.  
By correlating this information with previous presented Figure 6.7, it can be inferred that car-
bonation ingress will transpire at an accelerated rate. This aspect is essential for concrete ele-
ments intended for extended service life, as the carbonation will affect the service life. For the 
test elements in this thesis, it is determined that the relatively high RH does not induce any 
detrimental effects on the test specimens. The elements were covered until one day before test-
ing. Figure 6.11 shows the test specimens one day before testing.  
Figure 6.10: (a) Casting of mortar in the moulds. (b) When the specimens have been vibrated and lev-

6.4.2 Preparing for DIC  
2D Digital Image Correlation (DIC) has been employed to detect unexpected material behavior 
during testing and to identify potential sources of error. It is utilized as a technique to analyze 
and characterize the response of materials by correlating and comparing images captured dur-
ing test execution. DIC enables the measurement of displacements, strains, and deformation 
patterns, providing insights into the properties the of material.  
Some of the test specimens have been analyzed with DIC, the specimens have been prepared 
for that prior to testing. To gather the best possible DIC results, a black/white contrast must be 
created. An example of one test specimen ready for DIC can be seen in Figure 6.12.  
==PAGE:78==
Test of shear connections 
6.4 Test preparation 
66 
 
 
Figure 6.12: Test specimen sprayed and ready for DIC analysis 
The test specimen in Figure 6.12 shows that the surface has been sprayed white and afterward, 
sprinkled with black spray paint. The size of the black dots was iteratively tested to obtain the 
best possible results from the DIC analysis. 
 
6.4.3 Design of normal force setup 
During test execution, normal force is applied to half of the test specimens. In doing so, the 
influence of applied normal force can be evaluated and discussed. It is interesting to investigate 
the influence of normal force, as the deck elements are exposed to a frictional force. The normal 
force is applied with a magnitude of 10 kN.  
The test setup has been designed so that a force with a magnitude of 10 kN can be applied 
without causing unintended bending in the endplate and still ensure plane stress distribution. 
The design of the normal force setup was analyzed in the FEM-analysis program Autodesk 
Inventor Professional 2023. The design consists of two endplates, two threaded rods, and four 
nuts. The Inventor model is configured with 8.8 steel for the threaded rods, nuts, as well as 
steel plates positioned both below and above the specimen. The endplates are designed as S235 
steel and have a thickness of 12 mm. The loads in the model are applied with 5 kN in each nut, 
corresponding to a normal force of 10 kN. Furthermore, the model is fixed at the bottom sup-
port. As plate bending should be avoided, the model was analyzed with respect to principal 
stresses and displacement in the direction along the threaded rods.  
The FEM model was utilized to determine the normal stresses in the specimen by using the 3rd 
principal stress. The tensile stresses in the steel plates, resulting from the applied normal force, 
were evaluated by comparing them with the yield strength of the steel plate using the 1st prin-
cipal stress. Finally, the deformation of the plate was investigated to substantiate the stress 
analysis. 
Figure 6.13 illustrates the 3rd principal stress, which is the compressive stress. In the figure, it 
can be seen that the model is designed without threaded rods in the z-direction. By simulating 
==PAGE:79==
Test of shear connections 
6.4 Test preparation 
67 
 
calculations of the 3rd principal stresses it was experienced that the threaded rods were sub-
jected to compression in the model. This was not intended, as they were subjected to tension. 
By removing the threaded rods, the normal force was distributed as intended in the test. A 
control calculation has been performed by dividing the normal force by the cross-sectional 
area. 
 
σz = 
10 kN
160 mm ⋅100 mm  = 0.63 MPa 
(6.13) 
The normal stress distribution in the FEM model is validated by analyzing the 3rd principal 
stress, which is shown in Figure 6.13.  
 
 
Figure 6.13: Autodesk Inventor Professional 2023 analysis model showing 3rd principal stress 
 
Figure 6.13 shows a correlation between the calculated normal stress in (6.13) as the values 
are very similar in the interface between the two surfaces, thus, the model is verified.  
 
Figure 6.14 shows the 1st principal stress distribution illustrating the tensile stresses.  
==PAGE:80==
Test of shear connections 
6.4 Test preparation 
68 
 
 
Figure 6.14: 1st principal stress in the model 
 
The maximum tensile stress observed in the Inventor model is approximately 100 MPa. This 
is sufficient, as the end plates are made of quality S235 corresponding to 235 MPa yield 
strength.  
The displacement analysis can be seen in Figure 6.15, where the displacement is in the z-direc-
tion.  
 
Figure 6.15: Autodesk Inventor Professional 2023 analysis model showing displacement in the z-di-
rection 
==PAGE:81==
Test of shear connections 
6.5 Test procedure 
69 
 
Figure 6.15 shows the endplates have a maximal displacement of 0.2 mm. This indicates that 
there are no unintended deformations in the steel plate that would affect its ability to evenly 
distribute the applied normal force throughout the specimen.  
Since the 3rd principal stress aligns with the control calculation and both the 1st principal stress 
and the displacement in the z-direction are within the requirements, the 12 mm endplates used 
in the normal force configuration are deemed acceptable based on the model from Inventor.  
 
6.5 
Test procedure  
After 28 and 29 days of hardening for mortar and concrete respectively, the elements were 
tested. The tests were executed in an Instron 6025 test machine, which has a capacity of 100 
kN. A photo of the setup can be seen in Figure 6.16, where normal force is applied to the 
specimen.  
 
 
Figure 6.16: Photo of the shear test setup with normal force applied 
 
In Chapter 6.5.1 and 6.5.2, a description of how the test specimens were attached in the test 
setup as well as the test execution will be given.  
 
 
==PAGE:82==
Test of shear connections 
6.5 Test procedure 
70 
 
6.5.1 Attaching test specimen in shear and normal force setup 
The test specimens were placed and fixed in the shear test setup, as already presented in Figure 
6.16. A more detailed drawing of the setup can be seen in Figure 6.17. The specimen was fixed 
in the setup with tightened nuts ensuring the specimens were uninfluenced by any unwanted 
external forces.  
 
 
Figure 6.17: Test setup with normal force applied 
 
Figure 6.18 below illustrates the end of the setup both (a) without and (b) with normal force.  
 
 
Figure 6.18: Detailed drawing of the end test setup. (a) Without normal force. (b) With normal force 
 
==PAGE:83==
Test of shear connections 
6.5 Test procedure 
71 
 
The normal force was applied in the center of the specimen by tensing the threaded rods with 
5 kN using a spanner. The forces were monitored with a strain gauge on each threaded rod. 
These are shown in Figure 6.17. Specifications of the strain gauge can be found in Appendix 
Appendix E.  
 
6.5.2 Test execution   
To ensure the most accurate results, it was prior to the execution decided that the optimal exe-
cution should last 5 to 10 minutes. As the test specimens consisted of many different configu-
rations and thereby different failure loads, the applied load was monitored with a piston move-
ment rate of mm/min. Thus, it was possible to adjust the piston movement rate, depending on 
the expected failure load. The higher the expected failure load, the greater the piston movement 
rate. The piston movement rate for each test can be examined in Appendix F. As the test spec-
imen configurations varied between mortar types, reinforcement degree, and normal force, the 
piston movement rate varied between 0.15 mm/min to 0.6 mm/min.  
A datalogger was utilized to monitor the shear force, normal force, and displacement of the 
piston during the experiment. The measurements were measured in voltage (V), necessitating 
a conversion calculation to obtain the corresponding values in kilonewtons (kN). The input 
signal sent to the data acquisition system ranged from 0 to 10 V, representing displacements 
ranging from 0 to 50 mm and load values ranging from 0 to 100 kN. This conversion allowed 
for the accurate analysis and interpretation of the experimental data in terms of displacement 
and force measurements. 
In order to conduct the DIC analysis, a camera was employed to capture images of the test 
specimens at regular intervals of 5 seconds. Due to practical constraints, images were taken of 
all 18 test specimens from the first casting, followed by the selection of 6 specific test speci-
mens from subsequent castings. The camera was connected to a computer, and the images were 
saved in RAW file format to ensure optimal image quality. To perform the best possible DIC 
analysis, the grey distribution in each photo serial was enhanced to the best possible. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:84==
Test of shear connections 
6.6 Test analysis 
72 
 
6.6 
Test analysis  
The results obtained from the test data is evaluated and discussed in Chapter 6.6.1 to Chapter 
6.6.7. The analysis examines the significance of the various mortar types, reinforcement, and 
applied normal force while reviewing possible uncertainties. Moreover, the tests are assessed 
with respect to the constructional interface between deck elements and the intermediate grout. 
Various analyzing tools are incorporated in the analysis such as diagrams, DIC, and compari-
son with theoretical formulas.  
 
6.6.1 Analysis introduction  
 
The analysis commences with the presentation of selected load-displacement graphs. As 18 
different configurations have been tested, some selected graphs have been chosen. Afterward, 
the failure loads will be presented in a scatter plot divided into w/ (with) and w/o (without) 
applied normal force. In addition, some relevant normal force plots will be presented to show 
how the normal force develops doing test execution.  
The displacements in the x- and y-direction are investigated using DIC. This makes it possible 
to investigate whether the test specimens are subjected to rotation and how the normal force 
setup is influencing the specimen in terms of displacements. Moreover, the test results are 
compared with the underlying theory, while the mortars are assessed in the different configu-
rations in terms of being suitable to use in a structure while being applicable for separation 
using hydraulics. Lastly, the tests are related to full-scale DfD.  
During the data processing, it was observed that the results from the first casting differed from 
the results of the second, third, and fourth castings. One distinguishing characteristic sets apart 
the initial casting from the other three castings, as the mortar in the former underwent no vi-
bration during the casting process. The vibration could be the cause of the difference in results 
as the air content would be expected to be higher from not being vibrated. To explore whether 
there is any deviation in the air content, an additional test experiment was conducted to measure 
the density. The outcome of this experiment is presented in Chapter 6.6.5. 
 
6.6.2 Load-displacement analysis  
In this section, some selected load-displacement relation plots are presented and discussed. The 
selected plots have been chosen based on similarities and differences which are elaborated 
throughout this chapter. Load-displacement relation plots related to all tests can be further ex-
amined in Appendix F. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:85==
Test of shear connections 
6.6 Test analysis

Figure 6.19 below presents the load-displacement relations for the test configuration consisting 
of C100/400 mortar with 2xY10 reinforcement bars and no normal force applied.  
 
Figure 6.19: Tested load-displacement relation of configuration x.1.3 corresponding to mortar 
C100/400, 2xY10 bars, and no normal force 
The behavior of the load-displacement graphs is very similar across the four tests that were 
made where all have very ductile behavior with no brittle failure due to the presence of the 
reinforcement bars. Moreover, as the test is executed with deformation-controlled loading, ex-
erts an influence on the appearance of the graph. The max load is very equal for the four tests 
as they reach between 37 kN and 43 kN. 
All four tests show tendencies similar to the stress-strain curve for concrete subjected to com-
pression. They start by having a linear curve from low to medium loads representing the elastic 
behavior of concrete and then moving towards a non-linear behavior when reaching max load 
which simulates a plastic behavior.

the mortar and concrete. Subsequently, the test specimens exhibit a ductile behavior attributed 
to the presence of reinforcement, which is related to the degree of reinforcement, which will 
be investigated in Chapter 6.6.6.

of the mortar. The C100/400 mortar does have significantly higher strength parameters than 
the KC50/50 mortar. When the brittle failure exhibits in Figure 6.20, is due to the low strength 
of the mortar. Consequently, the load development primarily relies on the reinforcement alone.

KC50/50 mortar is further analyzed with 10 kN applied normal force in Figure 6.21 below, to

Initially in Figure 6.21, three of the tests show similar tendencies while test 1.2.5 deviates from

the peak loads are higher in Figure 6.21. This is due to the presence of normal force that in-
creases the friction in the interface between the mortar and concrete. The contribution of the 
friction has quite a significant impact on the capacity of the specimen as described in Chapter 
6.1. The material behavior is quite similar for Figure 6.20 and Figure 6.21, but the failure load 
has increased from approximately 6 kN to approximately 13 kN.

It should be noted that Figure 6.22 does not depict the failure of specimens shown in Figure

However, the observations from the tests illustrated in Figure 6.20 and Figure 6.21 indicate 
that minor cracks occurred at the interface during the initial peak in the load-displacement 
graphs. During the testing process, it was observed that cracks occurred in the interfaced at the 
same time as the initial peak was reached. This supports the notion of cohesive failure, indicat-
ing that the load-bearing capacity is taken over by the reinforcement reflecting the ductile be-
havior demonstrated in Figure 6.20 and Figure 6.21.

with 1xY10 reinforcement bar and 10 kN normal force applied as seen in Figure 6.23.

From Figure 6.23, the load-displacement curve for the three vibrated tests is very similar to 
Figure 6.21 in terms of a brittle failure after 1 mm displacement and subsequently a more duc-
tile behavior. A higher shear capacity is found for KC35/65 than KC50/50, likely due to the 
higher proportion of cement in the mortar mix. The first badge of specimens, which were not 
exposed to vibration, had a higher failure load as well as no initial brittle failure.

As previously described, the mortar from the first badge of specimens was not vibrated, unlike

investigated in Chapter 6.6.5. 
 
 
6.6.3 Effect of normal force 
A significant variance in the failure loads was found in Chapter 6.6.2 when comparing the 
relation between loads and displacement. This chapter aims to compare the different configu-
rations in terms of failure loads and how applying normal force affects the results. The average 
failure loads and related standard deviations are calculated for each configuration as listed in 
Table 6.8. Table 6.8 is an extension of Table 6.1 adding average failure loads, standard devia-
tions, and number of representative tests.  
From Table 6.8 it is seen that a total of four tests have been discarded, these tests have in 
common that they were the very first performed. During these tests, it was experienced that the 
failure loads were remarkably high. The loads can be seen in Appendix F. The test setup was 
investigated, and it was found that the specimens were misplaced by a few millimeters, the 
error resulted that the plate below and above the specimens were overlapping each other. As a 
result, the test specimens were not only subjected to shear but the part of the cross-section that 
was overlapping was also subjected to compression. The small area subjected to compression 
contributed to a significantly higher failure load, thus the results were not usable. Therefore, 
these four test results have been discarded.

The average failure load of each configuration from Table 6.8 is plotted in Figure 6.24 repre-
senting both with (w/) and without (w/o) normal force applied.  
Figure 6.24 indicates that an increasing amount of cement in the mortar increases the shear 
strength by taking the specimens with no reinforcement and no normal force applied into ac-
count. This does also correlate with the material parameters from Table 6.5. 
Furthermore, there is an increased shear strength of the specimens by increasing the amount of 
reinforcement, which correlate with the theory from equation (6.5).  
As equation (6.5) implies, the contribution from normal force increases the shear strength due 
to the added friction in the interface between the mortar and concrete.

presented. The value is varying between 0.9131 as the lowest to 0.9968 as the highest. These 
values are assessed to be reasonably high. However, it must be noted that only 3-4 tests have 
been performed for each configuration. Thus, one test result can affect the average value sig-
nificantly and therefore the coefficient of determination values must be treated with caution. 
With that in mind, it can be said that the average failure load is increasing linearly for each 
degree of reinforcement.  
However, the amount of reinforcement has a significant impact on especially the C100/400 
specimens. The capacity going from 0 bars to 1 and 2 bars is substantially increasing compared 
to the two lime-cement mortars. As the failure load is significantly increasing when using re-
inforcement in the C100/400 mortar, it may be a matter of mortar strength. As the lime-cement 
mortars have much lower-strength parameters than the C100/400 mortar, it could indicate that 
the lime-cement mortars fail to get the full beneficial effect from the reinforcement in terms of 
yielding. This is probably related to the degree of reinforcement meaning that the relation of 
steel per mortar area is too high for the lime-cement mortars. This will be further investigated 
in Chapter 6.6.6. 
Furthermore, the internal dispersion in relation to the test configurations is significantly higher 
when normal force is applied. A reason could be that the 10 kN normal force applied to the test 
specimen was developing throughout the test execution.  
To inspect the normal force development, two plots have been carried out presenting the load 
and the normal force on the secondary axis. They have been carried out from tests 2.3.6 and 
4.3.6 corresponding to mortar C100/400, 2xY10 bars, and 10 kN normal force applied are 
shown in Figure 6.25 (a) and (b). From the graphs, the force in each strain gauge is constant 
until the failure in the specimen occurs. From the failure in the specimen, the normal force is 
increasing until test completion. Especially load cell 1 is increasing significantly. As the figures 
also illustrate, the normal force is not increasing equally between the two test executions, as 
test 2.3.6, Figure 6.25 (a), has a higher normal force applied to the specimen during the test 
than test 4.2.6, Figure 6.25 (b). Therefore, the variation in applied normal force may affect the 
behavior of the specimen and thus the dispersion of results are higher than for specimens that 
are not affected by normal force

To investigate the influence of normal force and thereby higher friction, a bar chart has been 
created. This chart is presented in Figure 6.26. It shows the average failure load along the y-
axis for each test configuration. The black bars present the failure load without applied normal 
force and the red bars present the failure load with applied normal force. Additionally, the 
impact on the failure load is shown for each configuration.  
 
 
Figure 6.26: Bar chart showing average failure load increase for each test configuration when normal 
force is applied to the specimen 
 
In Figure 6.26, the increased friction has the greatest increase percentwise for the non-rein-
forced specimens. This seems reasonable as the mortars have relatively low shear strength and 
the impact of adding 10 kN normal force has a relatively larger impact. The pure cement-based 
mortar (C100/400) does not gain a high increase in strength from the impact of normal force 
while reinforced. The opposite applies to the lime-cement mortars, as they gain higher strength 
from normal force than from reinforcement. This is most likely related to their relatively weak 
material parameters. When evaluating lime-cement mortars, a tendency is observed in terms of 
adding friction to reinforced specimens. It is observed when the degree of reinforcement is 
increasing, the shear resistance is not increasing proportionally.  
By drawing parallels to a DfD scenario, several parameters can be considered. Upon revisiting 
Figure 6.20, Figure 6.21, and Figure 6.23, these graphs show the tested load-displacement re-
lation, and a common feature among all three is the initial brittle behavior corresponding to a 
cohesive failure in the interface, followed by a ductile behavior reflecting the impact from steel. 
Mostly, the highest failure loads have occurred during the ductile behavior i.e., where the im-
pact from steel is significant and less the mortar. As Figure 6.25 is based on the highest failure 
load for each test, it can be argued that the effect of the mortar is mitigated to a certain extent. 
In the context of a scenario involving DfD utilizing hydraulic pumps, the aim is to achieve

However, it must be mentioned when the specimens are subjected to escalating pressure, sig-
nificant cracks will emerge in the concrete, thereby facilitating movement for the reinforce-
ment. This phenomenon is presented in Figure 6.27.  
 
Facilitating the movement of the reinforcement could potentially constitute an opportunity to 
ease disassembly. However, a few tests were executed over a long time that the reinforcement 
detached from the concrete. Furthermore, the specimen presented in Figure 6.27 is only one 
configuration and it is difficult to assess the outcome for other mortars when loaded for long a 
long time. Nevertheless, it could be interesting to examine whether it would be possible to load 
the specimen such that the reinforcement facilitates movement and thereby eases the disassem-
bly. 
 
6.6.4 Deformation analysis with DIC 
Several DIC analyses have been conducted to detect small deformations that are invisible to 
the naked eye. To perform a DIC analysis, images must be of a certain resolution. Therefore, a 
Canon EOS 850D camera with 24.1 Megapixels resolution and an 18-55 mm IS STM objective 
has been used to monitor the specimens with a frequency every five seconds. In a matter of 
sorting, only selected DIC analyses will be presented in this thesis. In the DIC analysis, only 
the displacements have been analyzed. Optimally, stresses and strains in the test specimens 
should also be investigated. But when the analyses were carried out, it was experienced that it 
was not possible to analyze the stresses and strain. This is possible due to the frequency of the 
images, which must have been higher. It is assessed that for further tests, a frequency of 2-3 
seconds per image must be chosen, to analyze stresses and strains.  
By analyzing the displacements in respectively the x- and y-directions, it can be explored 
whether the specimens are subjected to rotation. Furthermore, the influence of normal force in 
(a) 
(b) 
Figure 6.27: Test 1.2.3 showing cracks in the concrete allowing for movement of the reinforce-
ment  
==PAGE:94==
Test of shear connections 
6.6 Test analysis 
82 
 
relation to displacements can be investigated. Therefore, three representative DIC analyses will 
be presented and discussed. One that is not affected by normal force and one that is affected by 
normal force. In addition to that, a similar test to the test specimen with applied normal force, 
but from another trial, will be analyzed. This allows a comparison between similar test config-
urations from different castings.  
The first DIC analysis that will be discussed is test 2.2.3. This test consists of mortar KC50/50, 
2xY10, and without normal force. In Figure 6.28 (a), an image of the test specimen before the 
test can be seen. Figure 6.28 (b) shows a picture of the DIC analysis. The color layer represents 
the displacement in the y-direction. The specific picture has been captured just after the failure 
in the concrete has taken place.  
In the analysis, six points have been added to the model. Three points on each side of the 
interface. Each point represents the displacement in respectively the x- and y-direction. When 
comparing the displacement in the y-direction, the y-displacement on the left side of the inter-
face is varying between 0.69 mm to 0.72 mm at that specific moment and the y-displacement 
on the right side of the interface is varying between 0.42 mm to 0.44 mm. The difference in y-
displacement on each side of the interface is due to the motion of the test machine. Considering 
Figure 6.28 (a), the support is on the left side of the test specimen. At the same time, the test 
machine is moving upwards and therefore pressing the left side of the test specimen upwards. 
Therefore, by subtracting point 4 from point 1, point 5 from point 2, and point 6 from point 3, 
the results will be how much the two materials have moved apart from each other at that spe-
cific moment. In general, the variation of the y-displacement on each side of the interface is 
very small. This indicates that the test specimen is not subjected to rotation and that the test 
execution has been optimal.  
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 6.28: Test configuration 2.2.3 corresponding to mortar KC50/50, 2xY10 bar and no normal 
force. Be aware that the displacement field is showing the displacement in the y-direction  
(a) 
(b) 
==PAGE:95==

6.6 Test analysis 
83 
 
Examining the displacements in the x-direction, the displacements are larger at the top than at 
the bottom of the specimen. Furthermore, the displacement in the x-direction in point 3 is neg-
ative. These factors do indicate that there is a very small rotation in the specimen - opposite 
what the displacement in the y-direction indicated. The inability to observe the slight rotation 
within the y-direction displacement is attributed to the very small magnitude of the rotation. 
The magnitude of the rotation is evaluated to be of such insignificance that its impact on the 
test execution is either non-existent or marginal. 
As Figure 6.28 (b) only presents the displacements at one moment, diagrams have also been 
carried out to present the displacement in the x- and y-direction over time in the six different 
points. These diagrams are presented in Figure 6.29. Along the x-axis, the test execution time 
is presented in seconds and along the y-axis, the displacement in millimeters is presented. 
 
 
Figure 6.29: Displacement curves over time in the x- and y-direction for test 2.2.3 
 
Comparing the displacements in the x- and y-direction, the displacements in the x-direction 
variates more compared to the displacement in the y-direction. Failure occurs after approxi-
mately 150 seconds corresponding to a displacement of 0.4 mm. The points are subsequently 
==PAGE:96==
Test of shear connections 
6.6 Test analysis 
84 
 
diverging considering the vertical displacement (y-direction) in Figure 6.29. From the displace-
ment equal 0.4 mm, the three points on each side of the interface are differentiating, where the 
displacement on the left side (points 1, 2, and 3) is significantly increasing and the displacement 
on the right side (points 4, 5 and 6) is slowly increasing. This correlates with the expected 
output, as the load is acting upwards on the left side of the specimen.  
Examining the displacement in the x-direction, the displacements are differentiating much 
more, as already described. This validates the assertion about a small degree of rotation in the 
specimen. It is further noticed that the largest displacements at the end of the test are the points 
on the right side of the interface (points 4, 5, and 6). The largest displacement occurred in point 
6, which is at the bottom of the specimen. During testing, it was observed that the failure line 
was greatest at the bottom of the specimen, which explains the difference in displacement in 
the x-direction.  
DIC analysis regarding two tests with normal force applied have been carried out. The tests are 
respectively 1.3.6 and 3.3.6. These tests consist of similar configurations, namely mortar 
KC35/65, 2xY10 reinforcement bars, and 10 kN applied normal force. The influence of apply-
ing normal force on the material behavior of the specimens will be discussed. The test speci-
mens with applied normal force before the test are presented in Figure 6.30 (a) and (b).

just presented for test 2.2.3. Thus, the x- and y-displacements are presented with six points for 
each test, with the displacement field in the y-direction as background. Both displacement 
fields in Figure 6.31 are representing the displacement in the y-direction. The analyses distin-
guish from the previous analysis by having a white area across the field due to the presence of 
the threaded rods.

The analyses show the displacement results right after failure has occurred. This can especially 
be seen for test 3.3.6 (b), where the white areas represent cracks, as the black dots have van-
ished. The white areas are also observed in test 1.3.6 (a), although not at the interface, but just 
above the bottom support. These areas are not cracks, but rather the concrete surface falling 
off. Besides the white areas, a patchy color difference is also observed, probably due to small 
fragments of the specimen falling off. The results in this area must therefore be treated with 
caution.  
Comparing the displacements in the x-direction at each side of the interface, the difference is 
so small that it is not noteworthy. This applies for both 1.3.6 and 3.3.6 However, the x-dis-
placement is largest in the bottom at the specimen for 1.3.6 (a) and smallest in the bottom at 
the specimen for 3.3.6 (b). Thus, a small amount of rotation is applied to the specimens, but in 
opposite directions. As the rotation is happening in opposite directions is most likely due to the 
location of the supports. Considering Figure 6.30 (a) and (b), the specimens have been placed 
oppositely in the setup, which probably entails the different behavior in terms of rotation. Gen-
erally, the specimens have been attached like 3.3.6 (b) in the setup.  
Examining the displacements in the y-direction, the displacements on the same side of the in-
terface are very equal. This applies to both tests 1.3.6 and 3.3.6. This emphasized that the spec-
imens have acted as intended doing tests.

the displacement for test 3.3.6 in respectively the x- and y-direction.

Figure 6.32: Displacement diagrams in the x- and y-direction. (a) and (b) is test 1.3.6 and (c) and (d)

As previously described, the results from the first casting have been different compared to the 
three others. Therefore, this comparison between test 1.3.6 from the first test and 3.3.6 from 
the third test is interesting.  
As for test 2.2.3, presented in Figure 6.29, the displacements in the x-direction, seen in (a) and 
(c), are varying significantly more than the displacements in the y-direction, seen in (b) and 
(d). Thus, the shape of the x-displacements is very different comparing tests 1.3.6 and 3.3.6. 
For test 1.3.6 (a), all displacements are increasing at the beginning of the test followed by a 
decrease. Comparing these displacements with the x-displacements for test 3.3.6 (c), all six 
points are constantly increasing, except for a small drop at failure.  
Examining the y-displacements, (b) and (d), they are behaving like test 2.2.3 (Figure 6.29), as 
all six points in each specimen are following until failure. After failure, the displacements at 
each side of the interface are following each other. However, point 6 in test 1.3.6 (b) deviate 
slightly, but that is due to the concrete surface falling off which has already been described. 
From the analysis of the load-displacement relations of the tests, it was observed that the spec-
imen from the first badge had different material behavior both in terms of more ductile behavior 
and higher shear capacity. The load-displacement relations including their corresponding nor-
mal forces regarding tests 1.3.6 and 3.3.6 is shown in Figure 6.33. Load-displacement relations 
for similar test configurations (2.3.6 and 4.3.6) were also generated; however, for the sake of 
clarity, they have been omitted. They have similar material behavior and capacity as test 3.3.6 
and can be examined in Appendix F. 
 
==PAGE:99==
Test of shear connections 
6.6 Test analysis 
87 
 
 
Figure 6.33: Load-displacement graphs in combination with the corresponding normal force along the 
secondary y-axis for respectively test 1.3.6 and 3.3.6 
 
The different material behavior is significant. Test 3.3.6 has a peak at a displacement of around 
0.8 mm, then lowers until the load is constantly increasing. Test 1.3.6 on the other hand is 
constantly increasing until the max load occurred corresponding to a displacement of 3.5 mm.  
It is evident to suppose that failure in the concrete for test 3.3.6 is occurring at the small peak. 
It can be substantiated by comparing with the DIC displacement diagrams in Figure 6.32 (c) 
and (d). To compare the diagrams, the piston movement rate [mm/min] must be known for the 
test, since the x-axes are measured in respectively time [s] and displacement [mm]. The piston 
movement rate for all tests can be found in Appendix F. Thus, for test 3.3.6, the rate is 0.4 
mm/min. Therefore, 0.8 mm corresponds to time equal to 120 s. Examining Figure 6.32 (c) and 
(d) at time equal to 120 s, the x-displacement turns out as well as the y-displacements as the 
specimen starts separating. Therefore, it can with great certainty be said that failure in the in-
terface occurs after 120 seconds with a 0.8 mm displacement.  
A similar examination can be done for test 1.3.6, failure is not immediately visible in the load-
displacement graph in Figure 6.33. Thus, by inspecting Figure 6.32 (b), the y-displacements at 
each side of the interface are separating. In addition, the x-displacements (b) intersect at time 
equal to 150 s. This may be a coincidence, but the shape of graphs changes clearly. Therefore, 
Figure 6.32 (a) and (b) indicate that failure in the concrete in occurring at time equal to 150 s. 
Knowing that test 1.3.6 has been loaded with a piston movement rate equal to 0.5 mm/min, 150 
s corresponds to a displacement equal to 1.25 mm. Thus, the concrete failure for test 1.3.6 is 
illustrated with a dotted line in Figure 6.33.  
As illustrated and described, it is not possible to investigate failure in the specimens from cast-
ing one based on only the load-displacement graphs. Why most of the tests from the first casting 
have different behavior is difficult to assess. As previously mentioned, the only difference in 
the castings is that the mortars from casting 2, 3, and 4 have been vibrated, which the mortars 
from casting 1 have not.

When deviating results were discovered between the non-vibrated first cast and the remaining 
castings, an additional test was carried out. It may be said immediately that a reason has not 
been found, but a description of the additional experiment as well as the results are presented 
in Chapter 6.6.5. 
 
6.6.5 Density of concrete cores 
The variation of the experimental results between the first testing and the other castings was 
investigated by conducting an additional experiment which was measuring the density of the 
specimens. As previously stated, the mortar from the first test was not vibrated as the second, 
third, and fourth test badge was. This seems counterintuitive as vibrating cementitious materi-
als reduces the air content and therefore a much more compact composition. Thereby, a higher 
strength after vibrating is expected. Therefore, the intention of the density study was to provide 
an explanation for the deviation of the primary experimental results. 
The density examination was carried out according to DS/EN 1036. Cylinders were carved 
from tested specimens measured 50 mm in diameter and a height of 100 mm. A picture of the 
concrete cylinders can be seen in Figure 6.34. 
 
 
Figure 6.34: Concrete cores with the dimensions Ø50 mm x 100 mm 
 
The specific procedure is briefly described in the standard DS/EN 1036, however a short de-
scription of the procedure was simply measuring the dry weight of the specimens as well as the 
saturated surface dry (SSD) weight both below and above water. 
The density results from the experiment are presented in Figure 6.35 below. The chart illus-
trates the density of each core, divided into mortar types. The red columns represent cores from 
test 1 and the grey cores represents test from one of the other tests.  
 
==PAGE:101==
Test of shear connections 
6.6 Test analysis 
89 
 
 
Figure 6.35: Bar chart presenting the density values for each concrete core. Note that y-axis ranges 
from 1500 kg/m3 to 1800 kg/m3 
 
Examining Figure 6.35, the calculated densities from the first casting are not distinguishing 
from the others and therefore it is assessed that the experiment cannot be used to explain the 
reason for the different results. It must be noted that the y-axis is in the interval from 1500 
kg/m3 to 1800 kg/m3. The chart shows further that the KC35/65 mortar has the greatest density 
while KC50/50 has the lowest. The difference in densities is not further analyzed or discussed, 
as it is outside the scope of the thesis.  
To conclude on the additional experiment, the densities do not show any difference in density 
between the different castings. Therefore, the air content cannot be used as an explanation for 
the deviation of the configurations internally. However, the air content in the concrete is not a 
definitive cause for the deviation of the results from the first casting compared to the remaining 
experiments.

with the impact of reinforcement. The theoretical bearing capacity has been calculated for each

and Table 6.6. The values and material parameters are listed in Table 6.9. The values of the 
cohesion and frictional coefficient represent a smooth surface cf. value from DS/EN 1992-1-1

6.6 Test analysis 
92 
 
In general, there are many uncertainties associated with this comparison. Partly, the applied 
shear strengths from Table 6.5 are uncertain since the values have been found based on a few 
experiments. Furthermore, the coefficient of friction and cohesion can also differ internally 
from mortar to mortar, as the composition of these varies slightly.  
As previously described, the theoretical bearing capacity is based on the modified Mohr-Cou-
lomb failure criterion. The formula refers to using the tensile strength of concrete/mortar, where 
the theory is based on cohesion as a direct reference to the shear strength. There are no stand-
ardized tests that can determine the shear strength of concrete, as there are many uncertainties 
associated with it. This is probably related to the fact that it is more conservative to use the 
tensile strength as long it is weaker than the shear strength. However, the applied shear 
strengths from Table 6.5 is obtained by testing in the same test setup as the tests in this thesis 
as shown in Figure 6.17 and Figure 6.18. This might indicate that these shear strengths are a 
fair material parameter to use for determining a theoretical bearing capacity. 
For the non-reinforced specimens, besides the C100/400 mortar without normal force, a gen-
erally higher bearing capacity is seen across all mortars compared to the theory. It may be 
related to the fact that the cohesion coefficient and the associated shear strength are not accu-
rate, even though casting, hardening time and testing have been carried out similar to Cømert 
[9]. Generally, the specimens with C100/400 mortar have Mohr-Coulomb material behavior as 
the theoretical bearing capacity is aligning with the experimental results. When considering 
Figure 6.36 and the results from the C100/400 mortar, there is a 15% difference from the mean 
test value compared to the theoretical value in the non-reinforced scenario. In this specific test 
configuration, only the cohesion and the shear strength are influencing the capacity which 
means either cohesion or shear strength is set to low. The value of the cohesion coefficient is 
as previously stated taken from the standard DS/EN 1992-1-1 + AC:2008 [53] corresponding 
to a smooth interface and the value is likely adjusted conservatively meaning the cohesion 
value could have been chosen marginally higher. However, there are many uncertainties asso-
ciated with an unreinforced experiment with brittle behavior. The percent deviation between 
the theoretical bearing capacity and the test results can just as well be connected to the execu-
tion of the tests and it would have been more optimal with a larger number of the same config-
uration to dilute that uncertainty.  
For the non-reinforced specimens with C100/400 with applied normal force in Figure 6.37, the 
test results are 93% larger than the calculated value. As the only difference from the previously 
described configuration is the normal force, the value of the friction coefficient might have a 
significant effect on this. The friction coefficient does influence the contribution from the re-
inforcement as well. This might be able to align the relation with theoretical bearing capacity 
and the test results, as well as higher quantities of tests, would. The normal force has been 
monitored with a strain gauge and is one of the factors with the least uncertainty associated 
with it. The frictional coefficient has a great influence on how much of the normal force is 
transferred into friction between the two surfaces. In Figure 6.38, depicted below, the interface 
is observed, where a smooth surface is evident on the vibrated concrete specimen prior to its 
casting in conjunction with the mortar. 
 
==PAGE:105==
Test of shear connections 
6.6 Test analysis 
93 
 
 
Figure 6.38: Image of the concrete surface before casting KC50/50 mortar 
The yield strength of the reinforcement is also a factor. 550 MPa is a characteristic strength 
corresponding to the 5th percentile of the performed tests. Only two tensile tests of the rein-
forcement have been carried out, presented in Chapter 6.3.4, to give an indication of the 
strength, but the quantity of tests is not sufficient to determine a mean yield strength on a sta-
tistical basis. 
The standard from which the theoretical bearing capacity is based on concrete minimum class 
C12/15 cf. DS/EN 1168:2005+A3:2011 H2.3, meaning that the lime-cement mortars have 
characteristic compressive strength way below the existing requirements. 
The tested bearing capacity of the lime-cement mortars in the configurations with reinforce-
ment is in most cases much lower than the theoretical bearing capacity. This is probably related 
to the fact that the mortar is too weak relative to the amount of reinforcement in the specimens.  
The relation of steel area per concrete area, ρ does not consider the relation between the com-
pressive strength of the concrete/mortar and the yield strength of the steel. If this was corrected 
in relation to the compressive strength of the mortar relative to the yield stress of the steel, the 
influence of the reinforcement would be visualized to a greater extent. This is also known as 
the degree of reinforcement, ω, and the relation is seen in equation (6.15) below: 
 
ω = 
Asfy
Ajfc
 
(6.15) 
In a standard scenario as represented in Figure 2.1 with 1xY10 per 1200 mm, a yield strength 
at 550 MPa, 220 mm HCS, and a compressive strength at 45 MPa gives a reinforcement relation 
equal to ωref = 0.004.

6.6 Test analysis
