that the experimental configurations are over-reinforced, which may explain why the test re-
sults of the lime-cement mortars do not reflect the theoretical bearing capacity as shown in 
Figure 6.36 and Figure 6.37. The pure cement mortar (C100/400) does have a much higher 
correlation between the theoretical capacity and the tested shear capacity than the lime-cement 
mortars do, as the cement mortar has a much higher compressive strength and therefore a lower 
degree of reinforcement. It is assessed that the specimens with lime-cement mortar do not have 
sufficient strength to induce yielding of the reinforcement due to their high degree of reinforce-
ment.  
 
6.6.7 Shear tests in relation to full-scale DfD  
This chapter will conclude the analysis by contextualizing the tests and knowledge conducted 
in this report with full-scale DfD scenarios. Furthermore, the tests will be evaluated in terms 
of improvements for future studies.  
As previously described in the analysis, the lime-cement mortars were unable to induce yield-
ing in the reinforcement to the same extent as the cement mortar. This is primarily because the 
two lime-cement mortars are considerably weaker than the cement mortar. The tested load-
displacement relation of the lime-cement mortars indicated an over-reinforced behavior since 
it overall reflected the behavior of steel with an initial elastic behavior followed up by a ductile 
plastic behavior. This was not the case for the cement-based mortar. This difference can be 
attributed to the higher strength and lower degree of reinforcement in the pure cement mortar. 
It is presumed that the inability to induce yielding of the reinforcement is solely attributed to 
the weaker strength and not due to the addition of lime in the mortars. Furthermore, during the 
investigation, another reason has been found as to why lime-cement mortars may not be ideal 
as mortar for deck elements. Lime mortars gain their strength through the process of carbona-
tion. As demonstrated in Figure 6.7, the carbonation proceeds at a slower pace as the mortar 
strength decreases. In other words, lime mortars become stronger over time, and particularly 
low-strength lime mortars take a long time to develop strength. This is not ideal in a DfD con-
text, as separation occurs many years after construction, and lime mortars will continue to 
strengthen, thereby complicating the disassembly process. 
Considering the tests conducted on specimens consisting of lime-cement mortar and reinforce-
ment, it is not definitely determined that it can be considered unsuitable as a replacement for 
grout. This is due to the significantly higher degree of reinforcement observed in the performed 
tests compared to a real DfD scenario, which indicates that the tests might not be scalable. The 
high degree of reinforcement is likely to explain why the test results do not align with the 
estimated theoretical load-bearing capacity described in Chapter 6.6.6. Further investigation is 
required, either through full-scale testing or by reducing the quantity of reinforcement in the 
specimens. However, the use of lime-cement mortars will complicate the construction process 
due to extended curing time and different strength development compared to cement-based 
mortar. It is advantageous for both construction and disassembly phases to achieve strength as 
quickly as possible, without significant further development over time. 
This leads the analysis to evaluate how the tests could be improved and identify potential areas 
of interest for future investigations. As it is assessed that only the pure cement-based mortar is 
==PAGE:109==
Test of shear connections 
6.6 Test analysis 
97 
 
a potential option as grout between the deck elements, it would have been interesting to test 
other pure cement-based mortars with lower-strength s. For instance, a potential approach for 
improvement could involve testing pure cement-based mortars with varying compressive 
strengths. The test could involve mortars with characteristic compressive strengths varying 
from 12 MPa, 16 MPa, and 20 MPa, in addition to the cement with the tested mean compressive 
strength of 32.5 MPa already utilized in the study. This could potentially lead to a more precise 
conclusion regarding the required strength of mortar for its application between deck elements. 
However, it is worth noting that this thesis solely conducted small-scale tests, which naturally 
have their limitations. 
Considering the opportunity of collecting data from full-scale tests, it could contribute to es-
tablishing an even stronger foundation for the implementation of DfD. This is because full-
scale tests would provide the opportunity to investigate shear failures in an even more detailed 
manner.  
Prior to the test executions, the hypothesis regarding DfD for concrete decks implied that em-
ploying a lower-strength mortar than the deck element would result in failure in the interface 
or in the mortar. This is also what occurred during test execution. However, there is a deviation 
from a real-life scenario, as the hollow cores of the decks are closed off with hollow plugs, 
resulting in a toothed interface between the mortar and the hollow cores instead of a clean cut 
as the case was in the tests. The hollow plugs in the channels can be seen in Figure 6.42 below. 
 
 
(a) 
 
(b) 
Figure 6.42 Photos by Anders Rafn:  
HCSs with hollow plugs stacked delivery (a) and in a project, ready for casting (b) 
Figure 6.42 shows yellow hollow plugs to prevent filling the hollow cores when casting the 
mortar around the HCS. The toothed interface, as seen in Figure 6.42 (b), can result in a higher 
load-bearing capacity of the joint of the mortar and deck, which is advantageous in the case of 
ultimate limit state failure. However, it also imposes greater demands on the HCSs during dis-
assembly due to a stronger joint.  
==PAGE:110==
Test of shear connections 
6.7 Sub conclusion 
98 
 
Furthermore, HCSs are typically only longitudinally reinforced due to being single-span ele-
ments. Therefore, transverse reinforcement is not necessary to contribute to the concrete deck's 
load-bearing capacity in terms of moment capacity and shear capacity. In a disassembly sce-
nario, the deck is likely not designed optimally for horizontal shear forces.   
 
6.7 
Sub conclusion 
Conclusion of Chapter 6, a significant amount of test data has been presented and analyzed, 
providing valuable information. The conclusive chapter does not only extend to the specific 
tests conducted but also considers a broader understanding of their implications for full-scale 
concrete structures. The key takeaways from the analysis are provided as a brief summary be-
low. 
• The initial part of the analysis presented and discussed selected load-displacement re-
lation plots from various test configurations. The majority of the tests exhibited satis-
fying results in terms of similar graph shapes and comparable failure loads for equal 
test configurations. Moreover, failure occurred in the interface of the two materials.  
 
• The tested load-displacement relations showed varying load-carrying capacities and 
material behaviors when comparing the mortars internally. The pure cement-based 
mortar showed a correlation with the stress-strain curve of concrete in the reinforced 
cases, which can be attributed to its relatively higher compressive strength compared to 
lime-cement mortars. Similarly, the reinforced cases for lime-cement mortars, particu-
larly the KC50/50 mortar exhibited material behavior like the stress-strain curve of 
steel. This observation also held for KC35/65 mortar, although to a lower extent, which 
can be attributed to the higher strength of KC35/65 compared to KC50/50.  
 
• Counterintuitively, the results from the first casting generally had a higher failure load 
even though they were not vibrated during casting, in contrast to the other three cast-
ings. Densities for both non-vibrated and vibrated specimens have subsequently been 
compared to investigate the difference in the failure loads. Due to the lack of clear re-
lationships observed in the limited number of tests conducted for each configuration, 
no conclusions can be drawn. However, further investigation of the influence of vibrat-
ing versus non-vibrating conditions could provide valuable insights.  
 
• DIC analysis was applied to investigate 2D displacements. The conducted tests revealed 
that the specimens exhibited minimal rotational displacement. For the load-displace-
ment diagrams without visible failure, the DIC analysis was used together with normal 
force graphs to examine failure time.  
Overall, the DIC analysis provided valuable insights into displacements, rotations, and 
failure behavior in the specimens. 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:111==
Test of shear connections 
6.7 Sub conclusion 
99 
 
• The initial theory addressing the shear capacity in the interface between concrete decks, 
induced by the modified Mohr-Coulomb failure criterion, was utilized to estimate the 
expected capacity of the experiments. The theory is based on cohesion, shear strength, 
friction, normal force, and reinforcement. Consequently, there are considerable uncer-
tainties when comparing theory and experimental results. Overall, for the reinforced 
specimens, the cement-based mortar correlated more with the theory compared to the 
two lime-cement mortars, which is related to the degree of reinforcement. The degree 
of reinforcement of the test specimens consisting of the two lime-cement mortars was 
proved to be over-reinforced, resulting in an insufficient capacity of the mortar to in-
duce yielding in the reinforcement. This resulted in a higher expected load capacity 
than the actual outcome of the tests regarding both lime-cement mortars in the rein-
forced scenarios. 
 
• The analysis was concluded by assessing the performed tests. The test configurations 
with the two lime-cement mortars were deemed to have an excessive degree of rein-
forcement, leading to the assessment that these mortars were not suitable for use in DfD 
scenarios. Furthermore, the literature indicated that lime mortars gain strength over an 
extended period, which is particularly significant for low-strength mortars. Hence, the 
lime-cement mortars are not ideal for DfD-scenarios. In contrast, the pure cement-based 
mortar appeared to some extent to be suitable for DfD scenarios. 
The low-scale tests were further contextualized in relation to full-scale DfD scenarios, 
highlighting how full-scale tests could provide a more realistic depiction of shear fail-
ures through the presence of a toothed interface.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:112==
 
100 
 
This chapter aims to provide a tangible evaluation of the practical implementation of the DfD-
system suggestion provided in Chapter 5 as well as an economic estimation. To assess the 
economic impact of the DfD-system, three different estimations are calculated as a tender for 
a reference building:  
• Demolishing 
• Demounting without design for disassembly  
• Demounting with design for disassembly 
 
The first method for demolishing involves the complete destruction of a structure by using 
heavy excavators to crush it. During the process, the reinforcement and steel are separated from 
the concrete. The resulting demolition waste is then collected and separated into small pieces, 
which are placed in waste containers. 
Secondly is demounting without DfD, in this method, there is no specific system incorporated 
for disassembling the structure. Instead, prefabricated concrete elements are cut free and de-
mounted from the structure. These elements are then directly reused by loading them onto 
trucks. 
Third and lastly is demounting with DfD, this approach follows the guidelines from the DfD-
system as presented in Chapter 5. Unlike the previous method, horizontal cutting of HCS is 
eliminated. Instead, a different work approach using hydraulics is introduced, ensuring that the 
structure can be easily disassembled, and its components can be reused efficiently. 
The expenses calculated in the tender are based on costs and manhours from the Molio price 
library [54] and insights from stakeholders in Chapter 3.3. This evaluation is an indication of 
the cost, as it has never been made before, hence there is no data to compare with. Thus, a 
comparative analysis will be conducted between a tender for an actual building, using the pro-
vided data in this thesis, and similar projects with available cost data. 
The final assessment concerning global warming potential, based on the literature analysis in 
Chapter 2.2.5, confirms that opting for reused HCSs is most advantageous. This sustainable 
approach was compared to existing widespread methods such as purchasing new HCSs, repur-
posing the project, or recycling crushed concrete for use as a base layer or aggregate. Reuse 
showed a 96% reduction in GWP compared to 79% for repurpose and 18% for recycling.
7 
Evaluation of DfD-system 
==PAGE:113==
Evaluation of DfD-system 
 
7.1 Related projects 
101 
 
7.1 
Related projects 
Due to the unavailability of other data, this thesis assesses and compares projects that are close 
to being similar as a reference point to establish some comparisons. The projects are elaborated 
on in this chapter and compared in Chapter 7.4. In practice, it is important to note that both 
higher and lower prices may occur, which can be attributed to factors not explicitly mentioned. 
 
7.1.1 Cost for new HCSs 
It is relevant to understand the cost of a newly made HCS. A report by Betonelement-
Foreningen estimates the price of different prefabricated concrete elements. The report was 
published in 2008 by Danish Industry [55]. From the report, the following two estimates were 
given, 435 DKK/m2 for 320 mm HCSs and 490 DKK/m2 for 400 mm HCSs [55]. 
 
7.1.2 Actual cost for reused HCSs in Norway 
An actual project named Kristian Augusts gate (KA13) in Norway by the real estate company 
Entra presents their cost for reusing HCSs [8].  The project managed to reuse a total of 21 HCSs 
with a thickness of 265 mm that was repurposed from the government quarter. The HCSs, 
measuring approximately 6.5 meters in length and 1.20 meters in width, were utilized on the 
top three floors (5th-7th floors) of the extension, covering an area of approximately 160 m2. 
The expenses for the reused HCSs involve cutting, demounting, testing, transport, engineering, 
and mounting again. The total cost of these expenses was evaluated to exceed the price of a 
new HCS by 5-6 times the cost.  
 
7.1.3 Actual costs for reused HCSs in a pilot project by Peikko 
Peikko, a supplier of composite steel and concrete structure systems, conducted a pilot project 
involving the installation and removal of HCSs. The project was small, measuring only 6 me-
ters by 8 meters with a height of 3 meters, and utilized 320 mm HCSs. [56] 
The HCSs were reinforced with perimeter stringers and grouted. After the concrete grout had 
dried the HCSs were cut free with a diamond saw. The project was deemed promising by 
Peikko and showed cost savings of 35%. However, it should be noted that such a small-scale 
example cannot fully reflect the reality that occurs in larger real-size projects. Therefore, it is 
expected that significantly higher prices would be involved in larger-scale implementations.  
 
 
==PAGE:114==
Evaluation of DfD-system 
 
7.2 DfD practically 
102 
 
7.2 
DfD practically 
As the application of DfD with grouted HCSs is relatively unexplored, this chapter focuses on 
a practical evaluation to assess real-life scenarios and investigate the process. The chapter in-
volves a visual examination of test specimens subjected to failure loads and gathers stakeholder 
insights to establish a reasonable building setup and demounting process. 
 
7.2.1 Evaluating failure-loaded test specimens 
Due to the limited experience in this area, a series of test specimens were loaded until complete 
separation occurred in the concrete to simulate the use of hydraulics in real-life scenarios. Mul-
tiple tests were evaluated visually with test specimen 3.1.2 depicted in Figure 7.1. 
 
 
 
 
(a) 
(b) 
(c) 
Figure 7.1: Test specimen (3.1.2) loaded to failure 
(3.1.2) = Concrete & C100/400 1xY10 with no applied normal force. 
The test specimens were examined visually and showed the formation of cracks that exposed 
the reinforcement, making it accessible for subsequent removal using tools such as a crowbar 
in Figure 7.1 (a), (b), and an angle grinder in Figure 7.1 (c). Comparing the outcome of these 
tests with the master thesis “Design for Disassembly of Concrete Elements Using Hydraulic 
Jacking” [10] elaborated in Chapter 2.3.1 proves a potential for the effective dismantling of 
HCSs using this approach. The thesis showed theoretically that HCSs can be separated using 
flat jacks and the photos in Figure 7.1 shows cracks forming beneficially. 
Note however, that full-scale testing would be reasonable to further investigate in order to 
check whether the HCSs takes permanent damage from the hydraulic dismantling, especially 
considering that the precast elements are hollow.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:115==
Evaluation of DfD-system 
 
7.2 DfD practically 
103 
 
7.2.2 Processes involved in demounting HCSs 
This section investigates the process needed to demount HCSs from a concrete structure 
safely and effectively. This is done by drawing on the insights gained through stakeholders in 
Chapter 3.3. The key insights are portrayed in Figure 7.2. 
 
 
Figure 7.2: Requirements for a safe demounting process:  
Multiple supports, 1 craftsman, 1 cutter, 1 rigger, 1 crane driver, and 1 manager 
 
In Figure 7.2 it is illustrated that a minimum of five workers are required to demount a HCS 
floor. This team consists of one craftsman, one cutter, one rigger, one crane driver, and one 
manager. Each role plays a crucial part in coordinating the demounting process and ensuring 
its smooth execution. It was also found that all elements need to be temporarily supported.  
In the process of demounting, a cutter and craftsman work on the top floor simultaneously. The 
cutter’s tasks involve cutting the grout between necessary elements including walls, columns, 
and beams as well. Meanwhile, the craftsman installs temporary supports and rigs the elements 
with chains to demount. The crane is operated by a crane driver who is communicating with a 
rigger whose main job is to unrig the elements and load them on top of trucks or flats while 
ensuring the trucks and waste is managed logistically. Lastly is the manager who manages all 
parts, this could also include multiple teams of the described composition working at the same 
time.  
The HCSs need to be removed using lifting equipment, which poses a challenge due to the 
possibility of the embedded ball anchors in the hollow decks being deteriorated after 50 years 
of use. Therefore, it is necessary to establish a safe and effective method for the removal 
process. In this estimate, soft fabric straps are considered a sufficient solution. 
 
==PAGE:116==
Evaluation of DfD-system 
 
7.3 Case analysis of building 310 at DTU

7.3 
Case analysis of building 310 at DTU 
The building in question is Building 310 DTU as can be seen in Figure 7.3, which consists of 
7 floors ranging from a basement store to 5 stories above ground floor having a total area of 
6,614 m2 and a height of 24.78 meters above ground floor. 
 
Figure 7.3: Photo of Building 310 at DTU [57] 
The analysis of the three demolition types should be comparable, thus a common ground is 
elaborated throughout the following next six subchapters. This structure was created to simu-
late common practices, following the construction manual [37]. 
• Tender: Establishes a common basis by specifying quantities and makes the bids com-
parable. 
• Rates: Refers to unit prices and unit time. 
• Building site: Includes a site plan, crane layout, and execution plan, considering the 
maximum number of workers that can operate simultaneously. 
• Time frame: Calculates total manhours need and provides a Gantt Chart plan that illus-
trates the critical path. 
• Construction site expenses: Breaks down the prices into different categories and ex-
plains the scope of each category. 
• Bids: Represents the final price proposal and includes an explanation of how the costs 
are allocated based on weight to ensure comparability across different construction 
components. 
Each of the following subchapters provides references to the relevant appendices. 
 
==PAGE:117==
Evaluation of DfD-system 
 
7.3 Case analysis of building 310 at DTU 
105 
 
7.3.1 Tender 
The tender is a structured document that outlines a contractor's proposal to undertake a project. 
The document consists of several sections that provide detailed information which is specified 
by the scope of work as well as the terms and conditions in this master thesis. The tender serves 
as a platform for the contractor to present their proposal in a transparent and lucid manner that 
enables the client to assess and compare bids. 
• The tender can be seen in Appendix G.1 
 
Scope of work 
The scope of work in this master thesis consists of a building site plan, and all quantities for 
the different building parts are measured from plan drawings.  
• All measurements of the plan drawings can be seen in Appendix G.2 
• All HCSs are counted in Appendix G.3 
• All quantities are collected in Appendix G.4 which are used as quantities in the tender 
in Appendix G.1 
• The situation plan can be seen in Appendix G.5 
 
Common guidelines  
The following common guidelines have been established to ensure that the three bids are 
aligned with each other. These guidelines help to establish a common understanding and agree-
ment among the proposals, thus making them comparable. 
• A 45-day time period for execution, with additional manpower as needed  
• The structure is cleared of interior, exterior, and installations, consisting solely of con-
crete and steel  
• The demolition ranges from the roof to the basement, excluding the removal of the 
foundation and sewer 
• HCSs must be demounted and quality assured for reuse 
The time-period was iteratively determined to align with the standard time for a full demolition, 
meaning that demounting should not take any longer time than the common choice. 
 
7.3.2 Rates 
The rates for unit time and prices are all derived from either Molio’s price library [54], experi-
ence from stakeholders in Chapter 3 or found in the existing literature. Molio is a reliable and 
well-established pricing database. Its price data is collected by a professional independent 
team, ensuring its accuracy and credibility. 
• The rates for the three demolition methods are shown in Appendix G.6

Figure 7.5 shows the given building site plan for demolishing. The building is divided into six 
smaller areas (marked with red lines) to allocate the resources, thus ensuring that two teams of 
three excavators can work simultaneously. Team 1 starts at A1 moving towards A2, and team 
2 starts in A3 moving towards B3, B2, and B1, thus working in a clockwise circle. The exca-
vators move between the building and the different waste contains to separate the reinforcement 
and steel beams from the concrete. The areas are of different volumes, hence the skewed dis-
tribution. The teams are allocated to work as far from each other as possible.

The building site plan for demounting in Figure 7.6 utilizes a tower crane (marked with a red 
box) and allocated areas for trucks to park near the crane. The choice of picking an optimal 
crane can have a large impact on the finances. For this process, both a mobile, tower and 
crawler crane was investigated with the tower crane being most feasible with a duration of 45 
days. The requirements for the crane have a heavy influence on the choice. The chosen tower 
crane is capable of reaching the whole building site. The heaviest lift is 15.9 ton wall with a 
reach of 20 m and a height of 36 m, and the longest lift has a reach of 38 m with a height of 36 
m.

appendices:

each other and that the available workers are increased to ensure that the timeframe is kept. 
Which is shown in the following appendices. 
• Gantt chart for demolishing can be seen in Appendix G.13 
• Gantt chart for demounting w/o DfD can be seen in Appendix G.14 
• Gantt chart for demounting w/ DfD can be seen in Appendix G.15 
The tasks in the Gantt chart are dependent on each other, meaning that the start or completion 
of one task relies on the start or completion of another task, additionally are resources allocated 
to the assigned task which ensures that no resources are being used simultaneously. Table 7.1 
below shows how the different types of craftsmen are distributed according to demolition type.

In the demolishing approach, a total of 7 workers are involved. This includes 6 demolition 
workers and 1 manager. When considering demounting without DfD, the total number of work-
ers increases significantly to 16. This is primarily due to the introduction of 12 cutters, as DfD 
is not implemented in this scenario, each element must be cut free. The need for 12 cutters is 
to ensure that the tower crane has elements ready for demounting at any given time. The need 
for a larger number of cutters contributes significantly to the overall workforce. On the other 
hand, demounting with DfD shows a noticeable reduction in the total number of workers com-
pared to demounting without DfD. In this approach, the total number of workers is 10. This 
reduction is mainly due to the implementation of the DfD-system, which eliminates the need 
for horizontal cutting. As a result, the number of cutters required decreases to 6, leading to a 
lower overall workforce. 
Overall, the most noticeable influence on the total number of workers is the presence or absence 
of the DfD-system. The use of DfD significantly reduces the workforce by eliminating the need 
for additional cutters and thereby streamlining the demounting process.

7.3 Case analysis of building 310 at DTU 
111 
 
It can be seen in Figure 7.7 that demounting w/o DfD is about four times more expensive than 
regular demolishing and demounting w/ a DfD-system included for HCSs is three times the 
price of normal demolishing. However, it is important to note that the HCSs have not been sold 
in the demounting cases, they are still a valuable asset. Later in Chapter 7.4 the sales price for 
the remaining reused HCS will be calculated in such a way that demounting will cost the same 
as demolishing. 
Figure 7.7 indicates that demounting with an implemented DfD-system can save around 25% 
of the total cost of demounting without DfD corresponding to approximately 1.5 million DKK.  
The cost for cranes and excavators increases by 70.000 DKK when the building is demounted 
using a tower crane instead of demolished by excavators. This corresponds to around 6-18% 
of the total costs.  
The cost for cranes and excavators increases with 70.000 DKK when the building is demounted 
using a tower crane instead of demolishing it with excavators. This corresponds to around 6-
18% of the total costs.  
Workers in Figure 7.7 are the main expense in all scenarios and close to 50% of the costs in 
demolishing and demount w/o DfD while only 33% of the cost of demount w/ DfD. This cor-
responds well to the number of workers needed to meet the schedule as presented in Table 7.1. 
16 workers are needed for demounting without DfD and only 10 are needed for demounting 
with DfD. 
The material expense covers rental personnel sheds for workers and tools, rental of supports, 
and the hydraulic compressor system. It can be seen at a relatively low expense and increases 
for each method as demolishing does not require support of each element or hydraulic disas-
sembly system. 
Waste management deals with the rental of containers, transport, and deposit of waste. In Fig-
ure 7.7 it can be seen that the cost for waste management is approximately 150,000 DKK for 
demolishing and increases by around 7 times. This is because waste management is the only 
resource that can contribute to an income. Reinforcement and steel are considered valuable 
assets as scrap, as they can be recycled with high demand. Concrete could also be considered 
worthy as scrap, but the market price for concrete is not as stable as reinforcement and steel 
beams, thus it is deemed necessary to be conservative and expect cost expenses to deposit 
concrete. However, it must be noted that the reinforcement and steel beams must be delivered 
clear of concrete within an index to sell it. For demolishing concrete, pulverizing is used to 
achieve this, as the building is being torn down and has an existing need to be sorted. For 
demounting on the other hand, a tower crane is being used that deposits the elements directly 
in the containers, thus the reinforcement and steel are not separated from the concrete. 
Quality insurance of reused HCSs consists of transport, supervision, administration, and stor-
age. The supervision is described in Chapter 2.1.3 It covers cleaning, sorting, and CE-marking 
of the HCSs, the cost for quality insurance is evaluated to be around 5-6% of the total. 
The last column in Figure 7.7 is the cost of running the construction site which implies setup 
of the workplace, operation, management, and risk management.

7.4 Price comparison 
113 
 
The total cost considered includes additional expenses when opting for demounting instead of 
demolishing for all structural elements. The following Chapter 7.4 calculates what the stored 
and reused HCSs from building 310 should be sold for demounting to reach the same total cost 
as demolishing. This cost includes the extra expenses for using demounting as a demolition 
method on all structural elements instead of demolishing. It is important to note that the dura-
tion of the demounting process can have an impact on the total cost. Therefore, to ensure a fair 
comparison between bids, the calculations are performed based on a fixed time period of 45 
days. 
 
7.4 
Price comparison 
For demolishing the HCSs are crushed and is an expense to waste manage. For demounting the 
HCS are quality insured, stored, and ready to be sold, thus the HCSs have not been sold yet.  
The primary focus of this chapter is to determine the selling price of the stored and reused 
HCSs from demounting. The reused HCSs are priced per m2 to match the difference in total 
cost between demolishing and demounting. Thus, demounting would end up costing the same 
as demolishing if the HCSs can be sold. 
By determining selling price from the difference in demolishing and demounting, it becomes 
possible to establish a fair economic balance between the two methods and ensure a financially 
viable approach.

7.5 What to do with the reused elements? 
115 
 
Table 7.4: Estimates and projects stages compared. 
 
As seen in Table 7.4 new HCSs ranges from product stage A1 to transport A4. Demolishing 
starts in-end-of life stage C1 and includes demolishing, waste management, and recycling with 
transport in A2. Demounting on the other hand ends at D1 the product stage as complete HCS 
elements are stored and ready for reuse in the construction stage. KA13 starts with end of life 
stage, with HCS being cut from an existing building, then processed, quality ensured, designed 
and used for another building project in the construction stage. The pilot project from Peikko 
on the other hand covers a very small scale but includes the price for construction stage A4 to 
product stage D1, note however that it skips the use stage, as it is only a pilot project. 
The total cost for demounting, as indicated in Chapter 7.3.6, is significantly higher than demol-
ishing. However, the estimated price at which HCS should be sold to cover the additional costs 
of using demounting instead of demolishing in Figure 7.8 shows promising results. Demount-
ing without DfD should be sold for approximately 1.5 times the price of a new HCS while 
demounting with DfD just below the price of a new HCS. This is a clear indication that the 
reuse of HCSs can be realized. Furthermore, the estimate falls within the range of the two actual 
projects, which suggests that the estimate could be reasonable. It should also be considered that 
DfD for several or all building components could make it even more cost-effective than using 
DfD on HCSs alone.

is a significant demand for a searchable database, similar to ordering new HCSs, where an

7.6 Potential market 
116 
 
 
 
Figure 7.9: HCSs Lengths from building 310 at DTU box plot with min and max = data range,  
x = mean, o = outliers, Q1 = lower 25%, Q2 = 50%, Q3 = upper 75% 
Comparing with Figure 4.7 (b) it was found that 75% of HCSs are more than 4 m long. This 
means that the decks in building 310 from Figure 7.9 are tilted towards having a smaller span 
length than the provided HCS data set. 25% of HCSs from building 310 are less than 3 m long, 
suggesting there might be less application for them in new projects. However, it is not impos-
sible if you incorporate them in the design, especially if a database is available. 
Moreover, it is worth reconsidering the concept of reuse by reimagining the construction pro-
cess as an alternative approach. HCSs could be repurposed as walls instead of in smaller resi-
dential construction ventures. Specifically, those with shorter spans of 3-4 m as from building 
310 at DTU or even lower once could be stacked on top of each other, with the hollow cores 
filled with concrete to enhance their structural integrity. This could be worth investigating. 
 
7.6 
Potential market  
Moreover, increasing CO2 requirements and the material shortage are part of the motivation 
for this study. However, a potential economic market could benefit from trading CO2 quotas.  
The calculations in KF22 from the Danish Energy Agency assume that the average CO2 quota 
price will increase from 606 DKK per ton in 2021 to 868 DKK per ton in 2035 with the constant 
2021 prices [59]. This indicates an increase of 43% over 14 years. Furthermore, a requirement 
for LCA calculation is introduced in 2023 for new constructions by the Danish Housing and 
Planning authority [6]. A CO₂ emission limit of 12 kg CO₂ eq/m²/year is imposed for new 
constructions over 1,000 m2 with an expectation to decrease to 7.5 kg CO₂ eq/m²/year in 2029. 
Resulting in a difference of 37.5% over 6 years. 
Thus, a project could potentially gain positive outcomes from trading CO2 quotas even if the 
first project to incorporate DfD in Denmark turns out to be more expensive than initially esti-
mated. 
 
==PAGE:129==
Evaluation of DfD-system 
 
7.7 Sub conclusion 
117 
 
7.7 
Sub conclusion 
The real project KA13 has successfully demonstrated the feasibility of reusing HCSs. The cost 
expenses for HCSs in that building alone were however 5-6 the cost of a new HCS. The KA13 
project covered only an area of 160 m2, but the estimation in this chapter covers a large-scale 
area of 6.614 m2 and shows promising results similar to the cost of a new HCS. It is however 
crucial to acknowledge that embarking on such reuse projects for the first time entails a steep 
learning curve, and the initial undertaking may involve significant risks. The estimation also 
underlines that DfD was only applied for HCSs, thus there are still benefits to gain from ex-
panding DfD to include walls and columns as well. 
Thus, in terms of cost, it is possible that reusing HCSs could prove to be more economical than 
purchasing new ones, particularly if a well-established CE-marking system and a comprehen-
sive database are implemented as assumed in this estimation.
 
==PAGE:130==
 
 
 
118 
 
This conclusive chapter presents the key findings from the thesis. The conclusions are derived 
from a practical, experimental, and economical perspective. 
➢ Multiple sustainable solutions were studied in a comparative literature review. When 
comparing recycling, repurposing, and reusing of HCSs, it was evident that the reuse of 
HCSs was the most favourable option among the three in terms of its impact on GWP. 
Reuse of HCSs proved to reduce GWP by 97% compared to using new HCSs. 
 
➢ Multiple disassembly methods were studied in a comparative literature review. It was 
found that DfD using hydraulics to separate HCSs grouted with mortars having lower 
strength than the HCSs had shown great potential in previous studies. The studies did not 
include reinforcement.  
 
➢ DfD using low-strength mortars in combination with hydraulics was considered fea-
sible based on the input from stakeholders and the literature reviews. It requires further 
investigation to assess whether HCSs are at risk of being damaged during disassembly 
with hydraulics. 
 
➢ Three proposals were suggested as DfD solutions to overcome critical barriers to the 
implementation of the reuse of HCSs. The barriers were discovered through a literature 
review, a comparative analysis of relevant stakeholder interviews, and an empirical anal-
ysis of HCSs. The first proposal was to incorporate a project for reuse as part of the project 
design. The second proposal was to implement the use of lower-strength mortars as cast-
ing between HCSs. The third and last proposal was to avoid reinforcement inside the 
hollow cores in the HCSs. 
 
➢ Test of shear connections was conducted to evaluate the impact on the capacity by var-
ying on different mortars, the quantity of reinforcement, and w/ or w/o normal force. Fail-
ure in all specimens occurred specifically in the interface, aligning with the anticipated 
expectations.  
 
◊ The material behavior of the different mortars showed variations in terms of duc-
tility and shear capacity. The cement-based mortar showed a correlation with the 
stress-strain curve of concrete, which can be attributed to its relatively higher com-
pressive strength compared to lime-cement mortars. The lime-cement mortars, par-
ticularly the KC50/50 mortar showed initial elastic behavior with peak load followed 
by a ductile behavior similar to the stress-strain relation of steel. KC35/65 and 
8 
Conclusion 
==PAGE:131==
Conclusion 
 
 
119 
 
KC50/50 showed relatively similar load-deformation curves. However, KC35/65 ex-
perienced higher load capacity compared to KC50/50, assessed from the increased 
content of cement. 
 
◊ In the absence of mortar vibration, the shear strength of the first casting was ob-
served to be higher than the other castings. Therefore, density tests were conducted 
to explore the variation in failure load; however, the results did not provide definitive 
conclusions.  
 
◊ Displacements, rotations, and failure behavior of the specimens were analyzed 
using Digital Image Correlation analysis. From the analysis, only small rotations 
were discovered in the specimens. 
 
◊ The obtained shear capacities were compared with the theory using the modified 
Mohr-Coulomb failure criterion. The specimens with cement-based mortar correlated 
with theory, while the two lime-cement mortars were much lower than theory. This 
is most likely related to the high degree of reinforcement of the lime-cement mortars 
since the lime-cement mortars failed to induce yielding of the reinforcement. 
 
◊ Finally, the lime-cement mortars are deemed unsuitable as low-strength grout be-
tween HCSs in a DfD-system. This is due to several observations from the tests. 
Lime-cement mortars failed to induce yielding of the reinforcement and the mortars 
keep gaining strength over time due to their chemical composition which counters 
the purpose of being easy to separate. However, there are no evident obstacles to 
using pure cement-based mortar, based on the findings from the tests. To derive more 
comprehensive conclusions, further investigations involving alternative cement-
based mortars of varying strengths, alongside full-scale testing, would be required. 
 
➢ An economic project case for Building 310 at DTU was calculated based on Molio’s 
available price data and inputs from stakeholders. The demounting method proved able 
to fall within the same timeframe of 45 days as demolishing. It was estimated that de-
mounting without DfD was four times more expensive than demolishing while demount-
ing with DfD was only three times more expensive. However, the estimation does not 
account for the resale value of the reused HCSs. 
 
➢ The sales price for reused HCSs was calculated using the economic estimate from the 
project case on Building 310 at DTU. To factor in extra costs related to demounting rather 
than demolishing, the pricing of a reused HSC demounted without DfD should be around 
1.5 times the cost of a new HCS. In contrast, demounting with DfD could be priced 
slightly lower than the cost of a new HCS. Thus, indicating promising results for the im-
plementation of HCSs in the circular economy. However, it should be noted that there 
may be associated many more unresolved risks, thus further data from actual projects are 
needed to give a more precise price indication. 
 
==PAGE:132==
Conclusion 
 
 
120 
 
This thesis ends by concluding, that DfD is considered largely feasible in the future, however, 
many barriers are still to be overcome. The test results indicate that the separation of HCSs 
grouted with lower-strength mortars including fire-reinforcement obtains a beneficial fracture 
without crack formation in the concrete for reuse. Based on a case analysis, it was calculated 
to be economically viable to use DfD principles on HCSs. The majority of the challenges were 
found to lie in implementing documentation and further developing the prototypes such as 
lower-strength grout and hydraulics on a larger scale.
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:133==
 
 
 
121 
 
Impact of hydraulic separation on HCSs 
It was assumed that separating HCSs using hydraulics on lower-strength mortars was possible 
based on previous studies presented in Chapter 1.  
This thesis has further investigated the shear capacities of test configurations with lower-
strength mortars. The number of required hydraulic pumps can be increased as required until 
failure occurs. However, it is deemed necessary to further investigate whether the HCS itself 
can be damaged by the applied point loads. Figure 9.1 and Figure 9.2 present some possible 
challenges associated with point loads on HCSs. 
 
(a) 
 
(b) 
Figure 9.1: Inspired by a figure from Betonelement-foreningen [35]: HCS cross-section visualization of 
point load from hydraulic separation. Normal HCS (a) and HCS with one casted core in the side (b) 
 
Figure 9.2: Inspired by a figure from BygGuide [60]:  
3D visualization of point loads from hydraulic separation 
From DS/EN 12390-1:2021 [49] it is known that applied pressure on a concrete specimen is 
most likely to form a triangular stress distribution in the concrete. Figure 9.1 shows a visuali-
zation of the horizontal point load acting on the HCS-element. Figure 9.1 (a) shows a likely 
challenge with the stress distribution being allocated in the hollow core of the slab. Figure 9.2 
shows the same stress distribution affecting the HCS in the span length. 
9 
Reflections
==PAGE:134==
Reflections 
 
  
122 
 
One possible solution to prevent damaging the HCS could be to cast the outermost hollow cores 
as seen in Figure 9.1 (b). Further investigation is required, such as numerical FEM-analysis 
and full-scale testing, to prevent damaging the HCSs. 
 
Other structural elements 
It was found when HCSs are extracted from a building, the other structural elements are re-
quired to be demounted as well. The influence of a DfD-system used on HCSs showed a re-
duction of 33% in the sales price of reused HCSs compared to HCSs without a DfD-system 
applied. This indicates that it would make sense to implement a DfD-system on the remaining 
structural element walls, columns, and beams, even if the elements are unable to be reused 
directly at the moment. 
 
Vibration 
A difference in failure loads was found between vibrated and non-vibrated tests as an unex-
pected, interesting observation. Since a reason for the difference has not been identified, it 
would be interesting to examine whether the difference in strength lies in the vibration or not. 
Such a study is out of scope for this thesis. However, the findings from such a study may 
potentially be of interest within the context of DfD.  
 
Test improvements 
Upon reflection on the conducted tests, it is evident that certain modifications could have en-
hanced the test output. Considering the significant variation in compressive strength among the 
utilized mortars, it would be appropriate to narrow down the range of mortars based on their 
compressive strength. As it has been described in the thesis, the use of lime-cement mortars as 
grouting for the purpose of DfD is not suitable. Among others, due to its significantly slow 
strength development. Therefore, testing pure cement-based mortars with compressive 
strengths of 12, 16, and 20 MPa could provide some interesting results as well as form a basis 
for comparison or even determine a lower index of suitable strengths. It would be interesting 
to compare their respective shear capacity with the corresponding theoretical shear capacity 
determined by the modified Mohr-Coulomb failure criterion. 
 
Full-scale tests 
As the experimental setup was a simplified version of the DfD scenario, there is a compelling 
case for further investigation into the principle of DfD for HCSs. The conducted tests have 
exhibited internal correlation in terms of configurations. Conducting full-scale tests would pro-
vide an even more authentic understanding of the effects of using lower-strength mortar.   
As the interface between concrete and mortar is smooth due to vibrating, the interface is sig-
nificantly different compared to real-life cases. The interface between a HCS and the grout has 
a toothed interface, which is why it would be interesting research to explore in full-scale testing.  
==PAGE:135==
Reflections 
 
  
123 
 
The data from the tests generally exhibits reasonable consistency. However, the number of 
conducted experiments for each configuration is not sufficient to draw definitive conclusions 
but rather provide indications. Therefore, it is advisable to perform additional tests of the cur-
rent configurations to establish a statistical foundation. Furthermore, exploring other configu-
rations applied to different levels of normal stress and amount of reinforcement would give 
additional data and opportunities to analyze their interplay.  
 
Implementation of DfD 
The thesis has found that further investigation is required to succeed with DfD on HCSs. Some 
of which engineers can derive from themselves and others that must be established by law and 
regulations. The areas that require additional work, include the following: 
• Experimental investigation of fire performance when increasing the spacing be-
tween reinforcement to 2.4 meters. 
• Advancement of non-destructive testing (NDT) techniques for non-invasive 
evaluation of HCSs structural integrity. 
• Releasing of a DS/EN to enable CE-certificating of reused HCSs in Denmark. 
• Assessment of alternative solutions for hammerheads, connections, and other 
construction elements. 
• Evaluating a workplace risk assessment for demounting of HCSs, which most 
likely requires cranes to suspend HCSs before cutting or using hydraulic pumps. 
• Establishing a common ground to clarify how and when stakeholders can bene-
fit from adopting DfD, including finances, reduced environmental impact, and 
other incentives. 
• Development of a centralized database for reserving and purchasing reused con-
crete elements.

Appendix A.1  
Interview: Nordkranen 
The appendix is made of the following page(s) 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:143==
Nordkranen 
Page 1 of 2 
 
Interview Nordkranen on 22/2-2023 
With Henriette Guldager Pedersen - Project Manager at the Property Development Company Nordkranen 
-Interview conducted by Anders Rafn in relation to Master Thesis at DTU. 
 
Nordkranen A/S Property Development Company develops properties and building plots, often large projects 
in collaboration with capital-strong partners, where they act as project manager. Nordkranen is experiencing 
greater and greater demand from their customers and partners in climate initiatives and increased focus on 
sustainability. Nordkranen has a mediocre interest in recycling concrete elements. It must be in demand by 
the client otherwise there must be either financial gain or a certificate on the circular action. Nordkranen is 
currently using the DGNB certification to meet customers' requirements. 
 
Nordkranen focuses on recycling materials in projects. For existing building plots where new projects are to 
be built, the opportunity is often sought to create sustainable layers with recycled crushed concrete, but they 
have not yet worked with directly reused concrete elements. Nordkranen is subject to the authorities' 
requirements which require a comprehensive amount of documentation to seek permits for recycling. 
 
Usually on large projects, the developer is in open dialogue with the contractor about how sustainable 
measures should be taken. They follow statutory requirements as well as the customer's wishes for DGNB 
certification. There is no money for further action. Nordkranen as a developer is willing to carry out additional 
green measures as long as they can assess that the end customer are willing to pay extra for a greener 
product. Nordkranen has an interest in characterizing their image with green transition. Thus, Nordkranen as 
the client on a building project has a great influence on whether to reuse concrete elements or whether their 
climate actions should take place elsewhere. They establish the requirements for the contractor to follow. 
 
Based on the Paris Agreement, a new EU Taxonomy Regulation has been adopted, which Nordkranen and 
other builders will follow in the future. It prescribes a certification scheme based on a classification system 
to document sustainable investments and requires how much green investment the developer has made. In 
this way, the circular economy and transition are made clear to investors. Nordkranen has a great interest in 
using this new regulation so that they can certify their sustainable initiatives and thus increase their image 
and satisfy customers/investors. 
 
 
 
CO2 consumption is currently not included in the bottom line of construction projects. Nordkranen has not 
examined financial business opportunities when reusing concrete elements yet. However, they could see a 
financial potential in selling surplus CO2 quotas, which may affect their sustainable initiative. 
 
==PAGE:144==
Nordkranen 
Page 2 of 2 
 
Nordkranen have a need for a searchable database that enables them to easily browse and select reusable 
HCSs in future projects to place orders, and manage their needs efficiently. This database should provide 
detailed information, pricing, availability, and other relevant specifications, empowering the client to make 
informed decisions and streamline their ordering process. 
 
Nordkranen sees the greatest risk of recycling concrete elements in connection with the disclaimer. Which 
stakeholder should bear the responsibility for the use of recycled concrete elements? There must be a special 
focus on that. 
==PAGE:145==
 
 
 
 
 
Appendix A.2  
Interview: Rambøll Norway 
The appendix is made of the following page(s) 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:146==
Rambøll Norway 
Page 1 of 2 
 
Interview Rambøll on 28/2-2023  
With Christian Gamst - Civil Engineer in Construction Technique at Rambøll Norway 
-Interview conducted by Anders Rafn in relation to Master Thesis at DTU. 
 
Christian Gamst is the primary construction engineer at Rambøll on the recycling project in Norway 
called Kristian August's Gate 13 abbreviated as KA13 developed by Entra. The project is Norway's 
first full -scale redevelopment, where, among other things, HCS where reused. As an engineer on the 
project, Rambøll is responsible for the reused structural parts. 
 
There was no standard for the reuse of HCS when KA13 started, and the control forms used for quality 
assurance of the reused HCS were therefore made project-specific in collaboration with the 
Directorate for Construction Quality and Sintef (the Foundation for Industrial and Technical 
Research). 
 
The existing HCS were not designed for separation, thus the hollow decks were stiffened with 
supports cut free at each end, then they were free to be lifted out with crane. They had to be cut 
vertically as they are cast into the joints. After removal of grout joint, they cut the hole decks to the 
right length. + cuts out slots, etc. In addition, workshop drawings were made for the new reused 
elements. 
 
At KA13, the reused HCS ended up being approx. 10 times more expensive than similar new ones. 
The economic benefit will probably increase for new projects, as the rules today are better adapted. 
It has not become usual yet to dimension with recycled hollow decks in the industry in Norway. 
Currently, you also do not look at design for separation. 
 
HCS’s are the most obvious concrete elements to reuse directly. Walls/columns and beams is on the 
onther hand very project specific in its reinforcement and geometry making them more difficult to 
adapt in other projects.  
 
Based on the project, Entra has made an experience report that has assessed LCA/EPD on the entire 
building. 
 
As a starting point, the engineer must be able to account for the full strength on the reused HCS’s 
after they have been through extensive and thorough control. But as this is the first project that they 
have built with reused structural parts, additional security factors have been added, so that a far more 
conservative strength capacity has been added. 
==PAGE:147==
Rambøll Norway 
Page 2 of 2 
 
 
Rambøll is interested in executing a project with reused elements again if given the opportunity. They 
see the biggest challenges associated with increased costs of transport and intermediate depots. While 
the greatest risk is damage to elements during disassembly and transport. 
 
The original building was separated by cutting the elements apart and craned out with customized 
lifting hooks. They could not use normal lifting yoke as they cast out with joint concrete as they were 
not galvanized. 
The major challenges of disassembling lie in coordinating with the demolition contractor and the 
builder, as there is no legislation to sell used HCS’s and thus there is a lack of CE certification. Here, 
special permission was to be sought from the municipality. 
 
The interest in reusing HCS is low at the moment for Rambøll, but they are willing as long as the 
customer pays for it. This is because the advisory fee is difficult to assess with these newly exploited 
ideas of reusing HCS’s. 
==PAGE:148==
 
 
 
 
 
Appendix A.3  
Interview: MOE 
The appendix is made of the following page(s) 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:149==
MOE 
Page 1 af 1 
 
Interview MOE d. 28/2-2023  
with Hans Christian Weidemann – Certified in Static Mechanics / Senior Civil Construction Engineer at 
MOE 
-Interview conducted by Anders Rafn in relation to Master Thesis at DTU 
 
If MOE should start incorporating reuse of HCS’s in their design, there must be either a requirement from the 
developer or authorities, or an offer of special savings on sustainability or finances. Pt. there it very little 
interest in developing design for separation. But if there is a reasonable basis and goal with it, MOE's interest 
will certainly be there as well. As advisors, MOE is most often interested in safe (financially) and well -known 
solutions. 
Hans Christian estimates that MOE as advisers has a great influence on design for separation. But on the basis 
of choices that are made very early - typically by the architect. It will be best when the architect involves the 
engineer in decisions about systems and the materials as early as possible. 
The biggest risk of MOE as advisers is unconditional - finances. MOE's profits margins are quite small in 
relation to revenue. So, if they make choices that affect the economy - just a few %, then the whole profits 
can very easily be transformed into a loss. Therefore, as advisers, you are typically a little conservative. 
It is believed that the biggest challenge lies in the development of the system: to find simple methods of 
separating systems that are otherwise designed against just being separated. 
A large one of the requirements set for coherence in the joint reinforcement comes from the robustness 
rules in DS/EN1992 and the fire rules for prefab-slabs. Hans believes that there may be a gain in finding 
systems that cater to both with a minimum of requirements for coherence. Eg. by dividing buildings into 
several, smaller main systems that can seem statically independent of each other and thus can be easily 
separated. 
 
When redesigning the joint reinforcement/construction details, it is recommended to be based in standard 
details, and supplement with those that can typically be found on the suppliers' websites. In addition, do not 
focus so much on recess details (since they do not have to be reinforced as much) and tire jumps as they are 
unusual. Peikko has also worked with some of the same concepts that can be used for inspiration.

Appendix A.4  
Interview: NCC Management 
The appendix is made of the following page(s) 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:151==
NCC Management 
Page 1 af 2 
Interview NCC on 22/2-2023 
With Jesper Smith Rasmussen - Coordination Manager/Project Manager at NCC Construction East 
-Interview conducted by Anders Rafn in relation to Master Thesis at DTU. 
 
NCC as a main contractor has an interest in being sustainable and promoting their image, they have internal 
departments in the organization that are looking for green solutions and certifications. NCC would like to 
participate in projects with recycling. NCC believes that the risk is low for those as a contractor, as they can 
just regulate the price as necessary, in addition to the high risk of the developer. 
 
It is estimated that NCC has no particular influence on the decisions on recycling, NCC would like to be green, 
but since the NCC is neither the client nor the developer they cannot make the decision. 
 
Jesper believes that decisions on recycling are politically determined and that parties as engineers, 
contractors and architects follow the decision of the client. The developer as well as project developers must 
come to the clients' wishes (typically pension funds), they have the final decision. In addition, it is a political 
decision whether the legislation should refer to a Danish standard to achieve CE marking of reused elements. 
If these factors succeed, NCC would like to do the work. It is difficult to combine both demolishing and 
demounting, you should only choose one solution. 
 
Jesper believes that we should be investigating opportunities for alternative business models for HCS’s and 
search for the one that requires the fewest possible processes. This is because every time one element has 
to be handled, it will increase costs, time and  CO2 footprint. You can compare LCA on HCS with reused HCS 
in different business models. In connection with the business models, one can investigate whether the 
contractor can handle the certification scheme directly out in the site after dismantling to obtain LEAN. 
 
The floor structure on top of the HCS is also a workflow to keep in mind. Foam concrete/subfloors could be 
a challenge, while Jesper suggests that using high water pressure jets with relatively low pressure will be 
plenty to clean the slabs before dismantling. 
 
Obviously, one must have a project designed for separation in order to achieve profitable solutions. Jesper 
mentioned some risks he would investigate. For example, if a supplier, let’s say Boligbeton, has designed HCS 
for a recycling project in 50 years, and Boligbeton goes bankrupt after 30 years, then it should be possible for 
another supplier such as Spæncom to certify the elements instead. As well, the business opportunities for 
reused concrete elements must be allow for competition, more actors are needed on the field to avoid 
monopoly of one supplier and contractor. 
 
==PAGE:152==
NCC Management 
Page 2 af 2 
When a project is to be designed for separation, one must look 50 years into the future which means the 
separation methods have probably evolved since. Thus, one must make it possible to separate via different 
methods, thus general principles must be thought out. 
One principle could be that one should avoid lying reinforcement inside the hollow cores of the HCS. This is 
up to the individual engineer to design. You can also think completely out of the box, where you cut the slabs 
off at the bottom of the wall collection, so that the joint is included as part of the hole decks as a new element. 
 
The HCS’s are typically never dimensional in a project where, on the other hand, other concrete elements 
are dimensional. But the requirements for fire reinforcement are strongly present and they are very extensive 
to change, it may be necessary to make large -scale changes. The same requirements have been used for the 
past 50 years, but there might be need for a change, however, this must be decided politically. 
==PAGE:153==
 
 
 
 
 
Appendix A.5  
Interview: Tscherning 
The appendix is made of the following page(s) 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:154==
Tscherning 
Page 1 af 3 
Interview Tscherning on 16/3-2023 
With Klaus Bodilsen - Contractor in the management of Tscherning in demolition 
- -Interview conducted by Anders Rafn in relation to Master Thesis at DTU 
 
Regarding common demolition: 
Regarding a normal demolition of a building, a contractor at Tscherning makes several considerations before 
the demolition starts. First, existing as built drawings are collected on the project, these are analyzed 
together with a counselor who prepares a stability project, this is a prerequisite for the project's completion. 
The stiffening project is about assessing the statistics so that the building can be safely demolished. This is 
done by maintaining stability during demolition using temporary supports, stiffeners and any fittings. For 
this, an APV is prepared which helps to dictate the workflow. This plan is based on a reverse order for 
mounting the building. In addition, the area is also examined; How high the building is and how close it stands 
to neighbors in relation to harassment and security. A demolition project involves large amounts of dust and 
noise. When the stiffening project is in place, the materials are documented to be recycled and a plan is made 
for these. For this, the structure of the building must be cleaned for accommodation, and pollution from the 
materials must be analyzed, especially for PCBs from paint and oil that can penetrate the concrete elements. 
When the structure is cleared and ready for demolition, a plan is determined to handle, this involves the 
choice of method such as cutting and chopping, as well as choosing machines. 
 
Like a regular building, a demolition contract can be divided into different forms of cooperation, such as total, 
main and professional contracts, Tscherning commits in all 3 types. It does not make sense to combine the 
methods. 
In relation to the choice of demolition method, several different ones are available, but most buildings are 
managed with standard or high-reach excavators. Out of these machines, 3 different is needed: one high-
range demolition machine with hydraulic drill or cut-arm to knock/chop concrete, one medium-sized material 
handling machine to crush the concrete and separate the reinforcement, and one smaller excavator to handle 
the crushed concrete and reinforcement. Here, most often a partnership is entered into with a recycling 
site/concrete crusher company to collect the materials. Currently you can handle approx. 90% of material 
from demolition in recycled products. 
 
Klaus indicates an approx.  
Unit time on a project in size: Ground area <1000 m2, height <30 m approx. 10-14 m2 dismantled/hour per 
hour with a demolition machine.  
Klaus also estimates a distribution of the total time on a demolition project that reads: 50% to prepare for 
demolition of construction at the same time as the savering and striping of the accommodation subjects, 
33% demolition of construction over terrain, 17% distant foundation/sewer under terrain. 
 
 
==PAGE:155==
Tscherning 
Page 2 af 3 
Regarding dismantling of whole elements for reusing: 
Tscherning has a great interest in recycling entire concrete elements and is already involved in dismantling 
concrete elements for recycling in the collaboration with the Technological Institute, where they provide 
their input to safe and sensible dismantling methods. Tscherning has little experience in dismantling and 
reusing entire concrete elements. Tscherning's influence on reusing concrete elements is limited by the 
customer's wishes and the norms. 
 
Klaus believes that everything can be done whether the concrete elements should be cut 100% out or 
whether they are designed for separation, the customer just has to be willing to pay for the product. Klaus 
emphasizes that it takes a lot of time for preparation as a salaried employee before the active demolition 
period starts. Here, one must also be aware that disassembling whole elements takes much longer than 
ordinary. Demolition, which the customer does not always understand, yet. Tscherning sees an advantage in 
that a project is designed for separation as it will reduce the complexity and there is clear evidence of what 
is designed for recycling and what the elements can carry. 
 
Klaus believes it is possible to use Flat Jacks for the separation, but he sees no resort about cutting the joint 
reinforcement. 
 
Klaus sees several major challenges, among other things, the norms of demolition and reusing cannot keep 
up with the development rate in recycling, which means that engineers must be far more involved and active 
in seeking different permissions from the municipality, which requires extensive documentation. This must 
be standardized as soon as possible so that positive competition can be achieved at demolition companies. 
In addition, there is a risk involved, as disassembling of elements is a new method that the executing 
craftsmen have zero to little training in, this means a great risk in handling the elements. You have to be able 
to CE-mark elements, while keeping the handling at the fewest possible processes, else finances and time 
can quickly overrun. 
 
Regarding design of new buildings with reusing elements: 
Logistics. How should one be able to incorporate recycling elements into a building? Klaus seeks a common 
website where a counsel/customer/contractor can advertise that they lack x-number elements of a type of 
this size at a desired time. This is a bit of the reverse tradition compared to ordering new concrete elements. 
The advantage of this is that a demolition contractor has the opportunity to dismantle elements for reusing 
independently of the builders' wishes, if they can see that they have the opportunity to sell them to a 
customer. Tscherning often finds that they are asked for recycling products in them, but they do not great 
opportunities as it is not announced. Such a website can work a bit like the tender. Klaus refers to Greendozer 
who sells surplus goods. 
 
 
==PAGE:156==
Tscherning 
Page 3 af 3 
In the case of reused HCS, it is also necessary that there is a common agreement on the quality of the product, 
this must be incorporated into the standard. For this, it could also be advantageous with a classification 
system. Since there is a big difference between the requirements for an element for house construction in 
KK1 and a storey building in KK3. In the case of HCS, the camber must also be assessed, as these are 
determined after a precise type of load and floor structure, here complications can occur when they need to 
be reused. 
 
Klaus suggests that you can also think completely out of the box and use hollow decks in smaller buildings 
such as walls or other alternative features. A small span HCS in a KK3 project could easily be transformed as 
a wall in KK1. 
 
Finally, it is suggested that a common database is established to check for HCSs that are in a project ready 
for disassembling, so that they can be ordered beforehand. This allows for the contractor to be aware of HCSs 
that can be of value and be sold, thus the contractor can start planning a project to be demounting instead 
of the contractor have to take the risk of demounting without a client being ready to buy the reused HCS. 
==PAGE:157==
 
 
 
 
 
Appendix A.6  
Interview: Boligbeton 
The appendix is made of the following page(s) 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:158==
Boligbeton 
Page 1 af 1 
Interview Boligbeton d. 17/2-2023 
with Tom Kristensen - Adm. CEO 
-Interview conducted by Anders Rafn in relation to Master Thesis at DTU 
 
HCS’s are produced on long assembly lines, they have project -specific reinforcement as needed. EPD is 41.7 
kg of CO2 per day. m2 but can be further reduced by project -specific agreements. 
Boligbeton is involved in a reusing project at the Danish Technological Institute in connection with achieving 
a circular economy. They are investigating how older buildings can be disassembled with HCS for reusing. The 
big challenge here, however, is that the drawings are outdated and not accurate, ie. it is not clear what 
reinforcement is in the different concrete elements. Thus, the slabs must be tested for stresses in 
compression, tension, bending and displacement. In previous trials you have had a passive environmental 
class, so you have not had to check chloride ingress and alkali content, you will need as soon as you examine 
concrete elements in other environmental classes. 
 
It will be very difficult to change joint reinforcement so that the HCS’s can be more easily separated by 
disassembly. This is because the fire requirements are very extensive and require a lot of documentation and 
test experiments. 
 
Boligbeton is interested in searching for circular economy, but they will look ahead. They do not believe that 
it is profitable financially to recycle HCS’s that are not designed for separation and reuse. This is due to the 
extensive test experiments that are required, as well as an exemption from the municipality for 
commissioning, and you do not have good case archives with drawings on old cases from the 70s eg. 
 
There is also skepticism about whether to reuse existing HCS’s (which is not projected for reusing) can be CO2 
friendly. This is because they have to be cut free, they must be handled during disassembly, tests and 
adjustments, as well as there is a lot of transport back and forth. 
 
Boligbeton could see itself treating reused concrete element, which involves that the grouting must be 
cleaned free from residual concrete, they need to adapt the HCS’s, create joint trails and certificate them as 
CE-marked. 
 
Boligbeton sees a great risk in reusing old HCS’s that are not designed for separation and reuse. But at the 
same time, they see great potential in designing slabs for separation and recycling so that they can be reused 
again. So, you have to look ahead and design the slabs up front with drawings and descriptions for which 
HCS’s are suitable for recycling, as well as its strengths. One should design both a building plus a recycling 
project.

Hilti

Interview Hilti Denmark on 17/2-2023 
With Mathias Nyawade Aaskov Wiuff - Key Account Manager

Hilti strategy goes towards a green future. You can buy or fleet tools at Hilti, Fleete is a package solution 
where, instead of buying a tool you can make a rental solution where Hilti is responsible for all service 
included in the package solution. 
 
Currently, Hilti has a strategy of 'Cradle to Cradle', ie. To recycle the vast majority of tools. Let nothing go to 
waste. Hilti collects tools and batteries. Some tools are donated to non-requiring use in connection with 
charity or education. Parts that live up to quality standards can be reused in workshops. What is left is sent 
to recycling partners. 95% of collected tools are recycled, including plastic and computer chips. 
 
They are developing a concept so that you can track what tools are using in CO2. It is a complicated calculation 
to assess how much CO2 is used by a tool, but the concept is under development. Tools at Hilti has changed 
to be powered by batteries, however, it is harder to calculated than tools powered by batteries than by fuel. 
This is because you do not know for sure where the power charging the batteries comes from.

Appendix A.8  
Interview: NCC Production 
The appendix is made of the following page(s) 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:162==
NCC Production 
Page 1 af 2 
Interview NCC on 21/2-2023  
With Daniel Lau Rasch -Labor leader at NCC in Bore/Cutting Department and BR-Service 
-Interview conducted by Anders Rafn in relation to Master Thesis at DTU. 
 
As a supervisor in NCC, tasks are primarily performed for NCC as a total contractor. Thus, as a supervisor, you 
do not have a special influence on which assignment must be carried out. Daniel is the supervisor in the 
drilling/cutting department as well as concrete and structures, thus he has great insight and influence on 
how the projects should be carried out. He is the last link before the executing craftsmen. 
 
As a supervisor, he will always assess the working environment and safety first. Then he will look into the 
task, ss the project profitable? All processes for execution must be considered; Material, hoist, lifts, tools and 
water/power, stiffening, cranes, lifting gear, dust/noise, space, moving gear and service lifting. 
 
When a building is to be dismantled, it is considered that a high-rise structure cannot simply combine both 
demolition and disassembly on the same floor. Either it is full demolition with bulldozer, wrecking ball and 
explosives or it is pure dismantling of concrete elements. Else, the risk for safety and damaging of the 
elements are high. It is not justifiable to demolish parts of the building while other parts must be reused as 
whole elements that are too many risks. Thus, it is necessary to do a cost/benefit analysis on the project, to 
assess which method is most profitable. 
 
If disassembly is to succeed, you must think of a safe and effective disassembly. As I said, if you need to 
dismantle, it is considered more proper to disassemble all the elements. Thus, in order to separate HCS’s for 
reuse, it is also necessary to disassemble walls, columns, beams and possibly toilet cabins. When 
walls/columns need to be separated, it becomes necessary to stabilize them again as they lose their stiffness. 
In addition, when you start removing all the elements, the floor is exposed so that the facades are without 
railing and there is a free edge. Usually, if a work exceeds 3 hours on one floor, you will establish railings 
around the floor, otherwise you will use fall protection. Disassembly just like mounting is a switching work 
where the workplace shape is constantly changing. Thus, it is considered the smartest to use fall protection, 
as elements must be removed continuously. 
 
When safety is controlled and stiffening is set up, the real work starts. How should items be dismantled? Pt. 
With existing projects where there is no designed design for separation, there are also methods, but they are 
very extensive: All the elements must be cut freely, in addition, it may be necessary to drill holes in elements 
to carry chains/straps through for disassembly. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:163==
NCC Production 
Page 2 af 2 
If one is to design items for separation with the least possible cut, the following alternatives can be used: 
• Vertical wall joints: Use horizontal stainless steel wall shoes (mechanical assembly) rather than locking iron 
• Horizontal wall joints: Use vertical stainless steel wall shoes (mechanical assembly) rather than corrugated 
pipes 
• HCS’s and bath cabinets with stainless lifting eyes 
These stainless mechanical joints must be able to be closed with a limb or similar. who meets the builder's 
wishes for architectural appearance. 
When wall shoes are loosened, it is expected to be weak enough to pull the elements apart with the crane, 
as the joint concrete has a weak tensile strength in relation to its compressive strength. One must include 
what types of floors have been established, as you have to secure a clean assault on the hole decks in order 
to grab the lifting eyes again. 
 
Assessment of unit rates on disassembly: 
• 
Setup, service lift, moving gear and safety = 5 min per element 
• 
Stiffening two supports in wall/column = 5 min per element 
• 
Cutting 1 meter horizontal (including setup and takedown of machines) = 20 min 
• 
Cutting 1 meter vertical (including setup and takedown of machines) = 60 min 
• 
Cutting 0.16 dkk/cm2 
• 
Drills 1-2 holes in wall/column for chains = 30 min per element 
• 
Setup and usage of Hydraulic System= 10 min per HCS and Beam 
• 
Dismantling of vertical wire box-connection between walls = 10 min per Wall 
• 
Demounting = 10 min per Element
